    title: Traducir 3
    draft: false
    Date: 2024-09-14T23:23:04
    description: "Traducir 3"
    slug: "traducir-3"   
---

# **134**

I followed the thread of magic power that had been attached to my shoulder.

If they chose to pursue, we would soon have each other in sight.

If they chose to withdraw, then I would become the one pursuing—either way, it did not disadvantage me.

I trudged through the rain-soaked forest, where droplets kept falling from the leaves.

Step by step, leaving footprints in the muddy ground, I swept my gaze around as I moved forward.

Then I spotted a group of four ahead—two men and two women—but the magic thread extending from me pointed in a different direction.

Snorting, “So you still won’t show yourself,” I ignored the foursome in front of me and kept staring down the line.

After standing there and watching for a while, an old man slowly emerged from behind a tree.

“…Alberto, this thing can recognize magic power.”

At the old man’s words—after he severed the magic thread from his fingertip—the spirit-sword wielder, Alberto, glared at the captain beside him.

“Just so you know, I only learned now that it can use weapons and recognize magic power.”

Saying that, the mercenary clicked his tongue like, “That’s cheating,” and raised his greatsword.

Because we were in the forest, this was not a suitable place to swing that weapon around.

That applied to me too—but I had not brought this out without thinking.

So I made them see.

Among the creatures classified as monsters, there were some that wielded weapons.

But they usually handled them crudely, relying on brute force—and they probably assumed the same of me.

To correct that misunderstanding, to make them properly recognize the threat in my hands—because I could not stand not showing someone the results of the secret practice I had kept up—I demonstrated a single swing poured with everything I had.

I cut through a tree in front of me—about as thick as the captain’s well-trained arm—with one stroke.

The tree collapsed, and the group’s faces twisted in shock, as if they finally understood my weapon was not just decoration I could not wield.

I prayed they were not looking at me like some dangerous lunatic going, “Whoa, this guy just started destroying nature out of nowhere.”

Anyway, I should act a bit more like a monster here and drag their thinking back on track.

“GRAAAAHHHHH!”

I leaned forward, thrust my face out, and roared to announce the start of round two.

I had been casually thinking up “monster-like” poses for a while, so it was probably perfect.

Alberto warned his allies—“It’s coming!”—but then a pointless thought popped into my head: “What happens if I feint and stop?”

Still, showing off this gear came first.

And I would actively use that comic-book swordplay lost two hundred years ago whenever I got the chance.

With a shield in my left hand, I could only use one-handed techniques, but in a forest, two-handed options were mostly dead anyway—so I would live with it.

My opening move was a huge, full-body thrust with a deep step.

An attack that would normally guarantee a counter the moment it was blocked became so powerful with this body’s specs that even if a counter was set, I could smash through it anyway.

I thought it was too fast for a human to handle, but he avoided it by throwing himself backward while bending his upper body far away.

The spirit-sword wielder was not for show; from that awkward posture, he swung up, aiming to destroy my saber.

Naturally, I still lacked information, so I pulled my saber back instead of clashing blades.

At the same time, a greatsword came down from my left—so I knocked it aside as if punching it with the tower shield.

The gap in weight and power was too vast; the captain went flying and slammed into a tree behind him.

First, I would receive the spirit sword with this shield—if there was no issue, then we could trade blows freely.

I stepped forward and closed in on Alberto, who had set himself to intercept.

Immediately, I felt as if weights had been attached to both hands.

Around twenty kilograms, by feel?

“Yeah, that doesn’t matter,” I thought as I raised my saber like nothing was wrong—then threw a sweeping kick instead.

“Tch! If you’re a monster, then fall for it!”

The “front” was an illusion made by magic, and the real body was slightly off to the side.

That old man would try to disrupt things the moment he saw an opening.

He liked underhanded methods, and given that only the spirit sword could land a decisive hit right now, it was a very rational approach.

So, I raised the threat level of the mage who kept vanishing again, one step higher in my mind.

Alberto revealed himself after dodging my kick by jumping back, and he slashed in.

I had been waiting for that; I received it with the shield, then tilted my head at how little impact there was.

It seemed my concern had been unfounded—the shield felt normal even after stopping a spirit-sword strike.

Judging that I could now clash with the saber, I forced Alberto back with brute strength as he tried to pour power in and cut down the shield.

He jumped back in time with my movement and managed not to be blown away, landing firmly.

I unleashed a flurry of saber strikes that practically said, “Time to punish your landing.”

The first was a light downward cut prioritizing speed—against a human, it would have been knocked aside, but unfortunately for him, I was a monster.

He somehow succeeded in deflecting it… and then I forcibly changed the trajectory.

A sweeping slash that could have split a torso in two was blocked by that incomprehensible defensive barrier of the spirit sword, but I followed through anyway and slammed both saber and Alberto into a tree.

Then I quickly drew the blade back and showed the same preparatory motion as my earlier thrust.

But just before I could fire it off, a thrown small bottle disrupted me.

I caught the bottle with the tower shield in a snap, and knocked down a flying stone spear with my tail.

They must have meant to cut in during that opening, but their timing did not line up; I blocked the mercenary’s downward strike with the shield, instantly knocked his arm up along with the greatsword, and slashed upward.

But just before my saber could reach, the captain was violently blown away.

That old man really did excellent work.

“Damn it! He’s moving his right hand like it’s completely healed!”

Alberto took distance while everyone fumed about my ridiculous recovery, buying time.

Facing him, I sent only my gaze toward the mercenary captain.

When I silently pressed, “Wait—did you seriously not tell them about the potion?” he smoothly looked away.

Oops. That would reveal I understood words.

Still, it had looked like a “probable noble” situation—and maybe I had been right.

Otherwise, there was no reason to conceal such vital information.

(Mercenaries move for money, but… did he cheap out too hard? Or is their relationship just that bad?)

I started worrying about their internal dynamics, but with the reset, they went into a brief “consultation time.”

They had decided to pursue after seeing my condition, but if I had already recovered, further fighting was too dangerous.

There were words I did not understand here and there, but that was roughly what they said.

I respected their judgment for considering withdrawal so quickly—but they should not think they could escape that easily.

I laughed in a way that sounded like, “GAH-HAH,” and the captain—who smirked back and tossed away an empty bottle—stepped forward.

Naturally, they healed with potions too.

Then we could still have plenty of fun.

Spinning the saber in my right hand, I began walking slowly toward Alberto, the greatest threat.

I watched him closely.

He watched me too, but if I asked whether those eyes held a will to fight, the answer was no.

He had clearly switched his thinking to escape, because even when I deliberately showed openings, he did not take an aggressive stance.

Or was he suspecting a trap no matter how much composure I showed?

If so, then he no longer saw me as “just a monster.”

That was a good trend—yet I still wanted more information.

Sorry you decided to withdraw, but you were going to humor me whether you liked it or not.

With Alberto still refusing to break his defensive posture, I stepped in even deeper—shamelessly close—and drew my saber back so far it nearly vanished behind my back.

A strike that was obviously impossible to block.

It forced a binary choice: “Dodge right or dodge left?”

I would leave it to your imagination what awaited on either side.

He could also gamble on a counter for a sudden reversal, but if he failed, he would die instantly.

Someone already set on escape would never take that risk, and as I brought my right hand down, he leapt hard to the left.

“Don’t jump, you brat!”

A warning came too late from somewhere—my right hand was not holding the saber.

The saber, wrapped by my tail around its hilt, shot out toward the Alberto who had jumped sideways.

I had switched hands at the moment of the downswing, planted my right hand on the ground, and changed posture for an ambush.

It was a perfectly lined-up strike, but as expected, that barrier-like defense from the spirit sword blocked it and the blade did not reach.

Still, Alberto was shoved in midair and bounced and rolled across the ground.

I dashed in for the follow-up, switched the saber back into my right hand, and charged forward like I was going to run down the captain who jumped into my path.

Something seemed to have been thrown from behind, but I was already gone from there.

The mage, whom I had thought was doing nothing, had apparently been trying to interfere this whole time; right after I felt my arms grow slightly lighter, she began chanting.

Alberto slammed into a tree hard, coughing out air.

That left the invisible old man… and if he could still turn this around, I almost wanted to see it.

The distance between me and the mercenary who had cut in between me and the spirit-sword wielder shrank.

A few more steps—just when I got that close, the mercenary in front of me made an unexpected move.

He reached behind himself and threw something he pulled out.

Not at me—he tossed it upward.

Spinning slowly, it entered my view.

A cylindrical object with a handle—matched something in my knowledge perfectly.

I slammed on the brakes with both feet, threw my saber backward, and raised the tower shield with both hands.

The unseen old mage spoke.

In sync with that voice, the mercenary hid himself behind his greatsword like a shield.

Then a metallic clink rang out as something struck my braced shield—

…and an explosion erupted.

The blast was strong enough to blow even this huge body away, producing exactly the result I knew it would.

(A grenade! Where did they get it!? How were they storing it!?)

Because it exploded at point-blank range—close enough to hit my shield—the shock was immense.

I slammed my back into a tree behind me and glared irritably at the burning scene ahead.

I had no intention of letting them escape now that they had started withdrawing.

But the moment I stood, I heard several small popping sounds.

I recognized them instantly.

Purple smoke spread around, hiding them from view.

And a brutal stench drifted in.

Not just a bad smell—something sharp and irritating, dangerous enough that even a slight breath suggested it could harm my breathing.

I looked up at the sky.

It was still raining.

Then the smoke would not last long.

(I could go around. But…)

A trace of magic extended from my side.

So faint that I would have missed it if I had not trained whenever I had time—thread-thin magic, like a spider’s silk.

(Did you think I wouldn’t notice? No—until now I could barely deal with that old man’s magic at all. So that’s why…)

“You got greedy,” I said out loud, laughing at their mistake.

I turned my back on the fleeing group to retrieve the saber I had thrown away.

I pulled the saber from where it had pierced an exposed tree root, and started walking.

“…Fine. Round three.”

+++

# **135**

At the end of the concealed thread of magic power I followed—there was only an ordinary tree.

In other words, they had simply attached the end of the thread to some random tree and used it to lead me around.

It might sound like an excuse, but even so, partway through I had noticed something like, “Wait—this isn’t the direction of town, is it?”

But if they fled in a straight line, their route would be easy to predict, so I started suspecting, “Were they trying to avoid the risk of being intercepted by taking a non-straight route?”—and that forced me to confirm the end of the thread.

(I should’ve cut the chase early and headed for town. That was the right answer.)

My physical specs were superhuman, but my intelligence was average.

I was terrible at deception games.

And yet—even from here, a superhuman monster could still turn it around.

Bad weather or not, I would find a four-person group dragging two burdens with them in no time.

…That was what I swaggered, and about twenty minutes passed. But I couldn’t track them by smell, couldn’t pick them up by sound, and a hint of panic started to show.

At this point, a suspicion surfaced: what if that troublesome mage was moving alone?

With two deadweights, even a normal retreat would get caught.

In fact, I had believed I would catch them.

So maybe they used that magic thread earlier to pull me away from the other four?

(If that’s true, then that old man wasn’t far from here.)

The possibility that they might actually escape became real, and at the same time I registered that the troublesome mage might be solo.

Who should I kill here and now—the mage?

Or the spirit-sword wielder?

The answer came immediately.

(Of course it’s the latter. Above all, I want to secure the spirit sword—the one thing that might be able to kill me.)

A powerful weapon stopped being scary if you took it.

There was a risk it could be taken back, but in this forest, finding a hiding place would not be hard.

A skilled mage was troublesome, yes—but still just one person.

Between a replaceable human resource and an irreplaceably overwhelming weapon, the priority was obvious.

My route should be to the town.

I headed straight there while staying alert and searching.

Right now, I had found not a single trace I could call theirs, and there was no denying the odds were against me.

Also, the gas they used during their retreat might still linger—and depending on its effects, the worst case, where I ended up in real danger, still couldn’t be ruled out.

Thinking back, my assumptions had been naive.

As individuals, fine—but as a team, their capability had clearly been thrown together in a hurry.

Yet once the lid was opened, if I excluded the two burdens, the remaining three absolutely had the power to kill me.

If that first ambush had succeeded, my head might have separated from my body.

(And what makes that possible is the spirit sword.)

I would take it no matter what.

There was no way there were multiple such dangerous things; if it was only that one, then taking it away was practically the same as neutralizing an entire nation.

And just in case, if I owned it and could hand it to someone who could use it more effectively, then even after I lost my reason, I could feel safer about what would happen.

I kept my honest thought to myself: “It’ll probably be an elf, and if I’m lucky, I might even get a nice deal out of it.”

Anyway, my attention was drifting into thought, so it was time to focus on searching.

After continuing through the forest toward town, I finally found signs that people had passed through.

Several sets of footprints pressed clearly into the rain-softened mud—probably four people’s.

I didn’t want to think this, too, was fake, but with something like this present, it was certain I was closing in.

Letting out a relieved breath—“So they really were heading for town”—I followed the footprints while watching for other traces.

After a short distance, my nose picked up a slight sense of wrongness.

A faint new scrape on a tree—fresh, with the subtle smell of metal.

(From the position, did it come from the greatsword the captain carried on his back? And if I factor in the number of footprints…)

Certain I could catch them, I smiled.

There was still distance to town.

For me, it wouldn’t take long—but carrying two burdens, it was nowhere near far enough to escape.

I switched from “searching” to “prioritizing caution,” and broke into a run toward the footprints’ end.

Then, right after I saw a wavering shape ahead that looked like a person—my foot sank into the muddy ground as if it were being swallowed.

“Gah!”

I steadied myself with the saber and shield and yanked my foot free.

Feeling something off, I turned—there was definitely a human presence there, even if I couldn’t see it.

I flicked a stone on the ground with my saber; it missed the mark slightly, but an old man revealed himself and said something—unfortunately, there wasn’t a single word I could understand.

It probably wasn’t the Kingdom of Canaan’s language.

(If that troublesome mage shows up here, is this meant to stall me?)

The old me would have assumed that and acted accordingly.

But I had already noticed two oddities.

Because I was keeping near-max caution, I could tell: there were two unnatural points where the sound of rain was clearly gone.

And the soundless ranges differed, with varying intensity—given his previous methods, one of them was probably a fake.

Meaning: a tactic that pretended to be stalling, while setting up a true strike.

(You think it’ll work twice…? No. That’s not it. I should assume they arranged it specifically to make it work the second time.)

If so, I had to consider the risk that both were decoys and the real thing was elsewhere—or that both had some kind of trap.

(This is… no—this is exactly what real combat is.)

There was too much to think about.

Up to now, I had been able to ignore all of it thanks to my physical specs—and now that alone wasn’t enough.

Then this was also a chance for me to grow.

I steeled myself, and a long roar thundered through the forest.

The moment it signaled the start of round three, I leapt at the mage.

A bold offensive, completely unlike my earlier caution.

(I’ll tear you apart—and make you my fuel!)

My first strike wasn’t with the saber, but a mass-based attack with the shield.

Until now, he had hindered my movements, but never “caught” them.

No—if I assumed he simply couldn’t, then this strike was useful for confirming it.

The old mage dodged my body-weighted shield crush with an effortless sideways float, then clapped his bracelet-wearing hands together hard.

A dry sound—then a popping blast rang right by my ear.

He was trying to steal my hearing, but my ears weren’t fragile enough for that.

For a few seconds I truly heard nothing, but I’d prefer he didn’t forget my regenerative ability.

I immediately shifted my focus to the unnaturally presence-less spots, but he had already moved behind me—so obvious it practically announced his aim.

Still, I had nowhere near enough material yet to determine which was the real threat.

Clicking my tongue—“He’s keeping an annoying distance”—I didn’t let up on the pressure.

Forward, forward—I kept pushing.

The fact he wasn’t using offensive magic meant I should assume he had no way to pierce my defenses.

(It could still be an act. But if I fear that, I’m playing right into his hands!)

So I closed in even at the risk of taking a hit.

While guarding my back, I prioritized offense and cornered the old mage.

Which meant I was forcing the others into a binary choice: “Abandon him—or save him.”

The floating old mage narrowly dodged a saber swing.

He kicked off a tree to change direction; I stepped in and smashed with the shield, but struck something like a lump of air—when it detonated, the shock blew him away like a dead leaf.

I pressed forward without pause—no distance for you.

I swung my weapons without pause—no time to chant.

My movements grew more violent, yet despite his frail appearance, he still let not a single hit land, fluttering and dodging like a leaf—and I couldn’t hide my astonishment.

The two presence-less things creeping behind me constantly hunted for an opening.

An unbelievable wielder.

A mage… no, a human could become this strong? I felt something beyond shock—almost admiration.

But defending forever had no future.

As long as I didn’t drop my guard, the real attack from behind couldn’t move.

They had clearly tried to act a few times, but each time, the old mage sent some kind of signal.

And that unnatural movement became openings, steadily accelerating the moment of his end.

As I swung downward, I read the terrain and moved to steal his escape options.

Just a little more… his limit was closing in.

If I continued like this—without ever weakening my rear guard—the end would come soon.

But my opponent wasn’t ignorant of that.

That moment would be targeted without fail.

If they hadn’t moved until now, then their moving at that timing was practically guaranteed.

So I would shift the timing.

That expected “final exchange”—the moment they believed they had lured out—I would not let them hold it in their hands.

Breath ragged, the seasoned mage kept reading my attacks and dodging.

Slash, thrust, bash, kick—he endured all of it.

His room to move shrank, and his motions gradually lost their sharpness.

Even from my perspective, it was obvious his stamina and magic were near their limit.

He used an exploding air burst to launch himself away from a shield bash.

I drew my saber back—then, at the motion that suggested I would add a thrust, the old mage placed his other hand against his extended hand and began a short, quick chant.

I stepped in hard, opened my mouth, and closed on him.

That conical barrier that should appear in front—one that had made my thrusts slide off again and again—

Before the chant could finish, I breathed fire from my open mouth.

Who could instantly judge that it was “something created by magic”?

He made the wrong call.

He reacted to the flame I spat—something that wasn’t even truly a threat.

That tiny opening was enough.

No barrier deployed, a solid feel of flesh on the saber thrust, sound and voice reaching my ears, and the scent of blood beginning to spread.

All of it told one outcome.

“… …”

I didn’t know what he was saying.

But he coughed blood, and the hand that caught it patted—pat, pat—at the blade that pierced his chest.

Then, gathering every last ounce of strength—

I pulled my sword out.

The blood-wet blade left him, and the old man drifted weakly through the air.

Blood gurgled from his mouth, and the tower shield smashed his body.

The presence-less things behind me still did not move.

An ending that deviated wildly from expectation kept them rooted in place.

I turned slowly.

The two invisible mists where rain should not have sounded began to clear.

With the caster gone, there was nothing left to hide the “real threat.”

I raised my saber and shield—

…and let out a stupid, empty sound.

“…Huh?”

When the magical concealment vanished completely, nothing remained.

I stared blankly at that nothingness for a long moment.

No one was there—so what, exactly, had I been guarding against?

“No way…”

I looked at the old mage lying silent on the ground.

“From the start?”

There was no answer.

In this place, there was no one left to answer that question.

“Was it all… a one-man act?”

The wavering I had definitely sensed behind me… the meaningless movements made in sync with it.

All of it had been to make me believe, “They’re there.”

“Why did you let them go?”

It was obvious he wasn’t from the Kingdom of Canaan.

He was probably a mage from the neighboring nation, Seizeria.

So why would he stake his life to let the spirit-sword wielder escape?

A corpse said nothing.

What did this outcome mean?

There was only one thing I could say: this old mage had probably fulfilled his role— and I had failed to achieve my objective.

+++

# **136**

I just stood there in a daze.

It was a complete victory—and also a perfect defeat.

(No… I don’t have time to think right now.)

I started running for town.

Betting on the slim possibility that “I might still make it,” I chased the remaining four with everything I had.

Even the clear evidence of footprints I had finally found had been washed by the rain and distorted, telling me a fair amount of time had already passed since they came through here.

Following the tracks, the road that cut through the forest came into view—and then I saw the town.

The gate was tightly shut, and there wasn’t a single person anywhere in sight.

“I didn’t make it,” I said, tilting my head back into the rain.

I sensed faint commotion beyond the gate and remembered my fight with that old mage.

“If I could’ve ended it faster…”

I muttered that, then immediately shook my head and rejected it.

The moment I’d fallen into his scheme in the first place, that possibility had already been crushed.

Attacking the town was not completely off the table.

But if I did that, I would be labeled an enemy of the human species.

Right now, I still hadn’t crossed the line beyond “a merely extraordinary monster.”

I had been careful not to cross it.

I had even restricted my actions so I wouldn’t hand them an excuse.

(I want the spirit sword… but if I act on that here, I’ll be seen as a monster attacking a town and become humanity’s enemy. That’s too soon!)

A town existed so people could live safely.

It went without saying what would happen if I attacked it.

(If they wanted a forward base for development, they should’ve made it a fortress…)

Then I could’ve handled it.

For example, literally wipe everyone out to prevent information leaks, or lure in a mass of monsters so it wouldn’t look like a single culprit—there were ways to stage it.

But with a town, the number of humans was on a completely different scale.

Let even one escape and it was over—and the risk of failure was far too enormous.

“There’s nothing to do but give up,” I reached the most reasonable conclusion.

But it was also true I couldn’t truly give it up.

The bait dangling in front of me was just that tempting.

I stood there for a while, growling, “Isn’t there some way?”—but every idea that came out was unrealistic.

(Since they clearly targeted me… there’s a high chance another extermination party will be formed. If so, do I prepare for next time?)

At minimum, the Kingdom of Canaan now saw me as something it could no longer ignore.

That was why they had sent a party after me.

The question was: how deeply was Seizeria involved?

Historically, these two nations did not get along.

(Did that old mage just happen to be there? No—Seizeria wouldn’t casually let go of someone that skilled.)

Was there some reason?

That doubt made me remember one incident.

(Come to think of it… was the human who suddenly appeared that night maybe the work of that mage’s magic?)

If I assumed that was true, only one answer surfaced.

That old mage would have been clearly betraying Seizeria.

(Would a mage betray a nation of magic?)

Honestly, “He was a hunter, so he came all the way to a neighboring country just to hunt me” made more sense.

If so, then that night had been the work of a different mage—meaning there were others who could do the same thing.

Of course, it might be neither, and frankly, I didn’t even trust my own hypotheses at this point.

“Thinking won’t get me anywhere,” I decided to return to the temporary base for now.

Ah—how enormous the prey I let slip was.

My steps were heavy.

Still, I soothed myself with the thought that I’d gained plenty of experience, and headed back.

Back at the temporary base, I groaned, squeezing every last drop out of a brain that hadn’t grown one bit despite my massive body.

If I had to summarize this incident, it was this: “Knowledge alone isn’t enough to deal with magic.”

There were many things you only understood by experiencing them.

On top of that, I had gained a general grasp of how weather affected things, too.

“Should I be glad I learned early that rain is more troublesome than I expected? If that’s the case, what happens with snow… snow?”

Something in my casual mutter caught, and I looked up at the rain that still hadn’t changed.

“…Oh. Come to think of it, there are less than three months until winter.”

It had already been about three months since I woke up.

In the Empire, winter began after the New Year, so these mild days were nearing their end.

(So it’s already that time, huh…)

Soon the temperature would begin to drop little by little.

I drifted into nostalgia—“Yeah, things like that happened”—then snapped back to reality.

“Huh? How am I supposed to survive winter?”

I had no heating—no house at all.

It was obvious food would also become harder to secure in winter, and I could easily picture myself desperately hunting every day out in the cold.

It didn’t take long to reach the conclusion of “This is bad,” and I finally began to recognize the problem that had been right in front of me all along and yet left untouched.

If I trusted the Empire’s science, it would be “Cold doesn’t bother me at all—worrying was pointless!” but this was my beloved homeland.

I could easily imagine them solving the problem from some completely different angle, like: “Oops! We made it resistant to heat, so it got weak to cold! It’s fine—wars slow down in winter anyway! Worst case, we’ll just put it to sleep!”

As someone who had been snoozing for about two hundred years, I wouldn’t even be surprised if they said, “Actually, you hibernate.”

I thought seriously for a bit.

Just how close to a “monster” had my ecology become?

And did the creature this body was based on actually hibernate?

Unfortunately, I had no idea what that original creature was.

But if I had to say what impression I had when I first saw this body—reptile.

(I should’ve explored that research facility properly! Not even knowing the catalog specs is coming back to bite me in the dumbest possible way!)

Clutching my head, I pictured a forum thread in my mind titled “Bad news: genetically enhanced soldier loses to nature,” along with the game-over music from some famous RPG and an image of my body collapsed on the screen.

I spent my days imagining, “If it comes to the worst, I’ll die in a cool way,” and yet dying like that was unacceptable.

“…Calm down. Nothing’s decided yet.”

First, a calm winter simulation.

Obviously, edible plants would almost completely disappear.

Don’t point out that I didn’t collect them in the first place—that’s irrelevant.

The important part was “they will stop being available.”

Naturally, if plants vanished, the animals that fed on them would vanish too.

No matter how I struggled, food scarcity was guaranteed.

My body didn’t seem to feel hunger easily, but considering the nutrition required to maintain this massive frame, I couldn’t afford to be optimistic.

Then there was the climate—hibernation seemed possible at the worst, but this body had never felt sleepy except in special cases, raising a very reasonable question: could hibernation even work? That let me shelve that worry for now.

And how much would falling temperatures affect my abilities? Even humans suffered changes from cold, so some change was inevitable.

The question was how severe—but I wouldn’t know without actually chilling my body and feeling it firsthand.

Depending on the result, I might need countermeasures like “wearing clothes.”

The more I thought, the more necessary preparations piled up.

If I went into winter unprepared, I could only see a future where I said, “Yeah… I still couldn’t beat winter.”

And then an important matter stabbed in like a finishing blow.

“…Wait. Does that mean my elf surveillance mission gets suspended too?”

The painfully obvious answer to my own mutter was this:

In winter, when the river froze—would elves still bathe naked?

(Of course not!)

After that internal Q&A, I slammed my fist into the ground.

I nearly crushed this shabby hut, but that didn’t matter—one of humanity’s hopes was about to disappear.

“So you bare your fangs… General Winter!”

If I were in the southernmost former Imperial territories, I might have more time, but unfortunately, the elven lands weren’t like that.

At this rate, the surveillance mission would not hold.

(Think! In what situation would someone take off their clothes in freezing winter cold!?)

Before I could even fully rev up this allegedly “mediocre” brain, the answer came immediately.

A bath.

But even before asking whether I could prepare such a thing—who would ever come bathe in a bath prepared by a monster?

Number Six might be possible, and I did have someone else in mind who I could probably talk into it.

But think about it.

Where would I even set it up?

If the distance was far, it was pointless.

So the answer I reached was: a hot spring.

In the southwest of the Empire, there was definitely a leisure-focused district with hot spring inns, and it was obvious the source was somewhere in the nearby mountain range.

But again, the problem was distance.

If I followed the river, I could get there in under half a day—but could an elf do that? Unless they were extremely trained, probably not.

Then I needed another candidate location.

Sadly, my knowledge didn’t contain any other place where I could find a hot spring.

More precisely, “within the former Imperial territories”—and mountains on the Canaan–Seizeria border in the opposite direction were out of the question.

(Do I take the risk and enter elven territory? No. I’m pretty sure there’s some kind of magical alarm system that would give me away.)

There simply wasn’t any suitable location.

Just as I slumped, thinking, “This plan is dead,” a revelation descended.

“Right. New development.”

I had been too obsessed because she was such an exceptional “resource.”

To solve the distance problem, I would change my target to elves closer by.

Scoffing at myself—“How did I not realize this?”—I slowly stood up.

I damaged the hut’s ceiling a bit, but I didn’t need this place anymore.

The spirit sword was a shame, but there was no need to take unnecessary risk—and I should prioritize preparing for a winter I’d never experienced in this form.

Part of that was because I’d forgotten the possibility that I might be weak to cold, but this was an action prioritizing survival.

If I wanted to prepare for the worst, it was never “too early” to start moving now.

Because I didn’t know what would happen, I needed to aim for a fully prepared state.

…And while saying all that, I finished packing with a spring in my step, thinking, “A new encounter is waiting for me.”

Without noticing that my premise was wildly mistaken, I slung my gear over my back and ran through the forest.

Thanks to the muddy ground, I couldn’t move as fast as usual.

I noted that as yet another point to watch—and my thoughts wandered.

And since I was heading into an unexplored region, I also realized there was a chance of finding a research facility, or buildings and underground spaces usable as a main base. I started praising myself so hard that I’ll add this too: once that started, I couldn’t even correct my course properly.

+++

## **137 - A Certain Mage’s Soliloquy**

How long has it been since “Kids these days…” started slipping out of my mouth like a habit?

Come to think of it, ever since my talent for the arcane was discovered, I had spent my days doing nothing but honing myself.

For a time, I took pride in my gifts and carried myself with arrogance… but after I met someone truly worthy of being called a genius—and had my nose thoroughly broken—I felt like I did nothing but train and train.

Back then, I believed in my own talent. I mistakenly thought that if I worked hard, I would surely reach the top.

In the end, I became nothing at all, and as if to avert my eyes from that fact, I threw myself into a single thing.

Supportive magic that nobody cared about—something that wasn’t even considered “proper sorcery.” Specialists in it had long since vanished, and it was the perfect refuge for someone like me.

But then an unexpected truth came to light.

Tragically, I realized how small my talent for true sorcery really was—and at the same time, I became aware that I had an aptitude for combat.

“I chose the wrong path.”

That was the only way to describe it.

By the time I had passed fifty and my efforts finally took shape, it was already too late.

In Seizeria, what earned esteem was sorcery, not magic.

Our nation valued sorcery, whose results varied greatly depending on the wielder, over magic, which produced almost the same effect no matter who used it.

From the standpoint of statecraft, that judgment was correct.

Because we had always been threatened not only by a powerful neighbor, but also by monsters, what the country needed was not a multitude of spellcasters, but a single elite sorcerer.

A single overwhelming individual could scatter even an army; that threat became deterrence and tilted diplomacy in our favor.

And when war did come, history proved time and again that such power, displayed in full, could lead one’s side to victory.

Even knowing that, I sought an escape in “magic” because I wanted to believe I still had talent.

But reality was cruel.

So I kept forging myself, kept showing results—thinking I could make them regret their scorn.

The outcome goes without saying.

The masses, and even those involved with sorcery—nay, even nobles and researchers—came to prefer flashy things, neglecting the plain but highly useful arts of magic.

“Isn’t it something you just pick up as a bonus?”

The moment I realized my specialty—my skills and knowledge—would never be spoken of as anything more than that… and understood that my efforts would change nothing…

After that, things moved quickly.

Before I knew it, I had left the country.

Conveniently, a neighboring nation was recruiting skilled sorcerers.

If I went somewhere untouched by Seizeria’s assumptions and values, would I receive proper recognition?

That thought drew me in.

What awaited me there was a crowd of sorcerers.

Given that the recruiter was a marquis’s house in the Kingdom of Canaan, that was only natural.

Still, the sight was rare by Seizerian standards. I was shocked—and I sneered at colleagues swarming for money and status—then remembered I was one of them and sneered at myself.

Even so, it was a good chance to learn the caliber of other nations’ sorcerers.

If you were not greedy for knowledge and experience, you had no right to call yourself a sorcerer.

The problem was that the quality of those who gathered was far worse than I had expected.

“…Surely you aren’t going to tell me it’s already over?”

I spat the words at the dozen-odd sorcerers who couldn’t even manage to stand at the center with me.

One-on-one, they were no match.

So I increased the numbers… and this was the result.

I was tired, yes, but even when I faced them all at once at the end, they were quite literally “no match.”

I couldn’t help letting out a sigh at how low Canaan’s sorcerous standards were, but the marquis seemed extremely satisfied, and even proposed doubling my initial pay.

Recognition, in any form, was something I had been starving for—so I was deeply pleased.

After that, I took on various errands and jobs, and one day I was introduced to a young man.

“My name is Alberto. I look forward to working with you.”

At first glance he was just a handsome youth, but listening to him, I learned he was a prodigy of the sword.

Genius—there was much I could say about that word, but he wasn’t warped in personality; he seemed like an earnest, decent young man who trained seriously.

He appeared to worry that he looked younger than he was, but to me he was still nothing but a youngster.

And it seemed they intended to pit this boy against a dragon that had appeared in the north.

So that’s why they needed a sorcerer—I grasped the marquis’s aim.

But that meant they had foreseen the dragon’s attack.

(Or they have a way to call it in…)

Naturally, I am not such a fool as to stick my nose into something so dangerous.

A hero manufactured by human design.

Even a sorcery-addled man like me understood what that meant.

But having already left my homeland, I had no intention of stopping it, either.

How it would all turn out was not something someone like me should be thinking about.

Before I knew it, preparations were complete, and the party drove north in their carriage.

In the end, one could say the plan was a great success.

They paid a heavy price, but they managed to drive the dragon away—and the boy’s skill in snatching the final blow was the real thing.

And even after taking that glory, he bowed his head and said, “It was only possible because of your assistance,” which did not feel unpleasant at all.

How many people could say that sincerely?

I had been impressed—thinking him a youngster with promise—but it seemed his true goal was instruction in sorcery.

Despite being discovered young by a marquis, he understood how to navigate the world.

But alas, no matter how fervently he begged for teaching, what mattered was talent.

And by “talent,” I meant a prerequisite—meaning that no matter how much he trained, he would never be able to wield sorcery.

Many people thought, “Maybe…” but I did not know whether anyone had ever made that hope real.

It wasn’t something I cared to find out, so I made him give up early.

Still, his attitude—“Knowledge will be useful for something”—was worthy of praise.

“From now on I’m a dragonslayer. Someday they’ll call me a hero. It’d be embarrassing if I don’t know things, right?”

It was the first time I had met someone who learned arcane knowledge based on whether it looked cool.

(He may become a true big shot, surprisingly.)

That day, I laughed until I hurt.

It had been a very long time since I laughed like that… but either way, it felt good.

In the gaps between work, I taught the boy what I knew.

He struggled to get used to the new sword he had obtained, but my teaching was not gentle enough to let him slack off because of that.

And then—this happened.

The weapon the boy had newly acquired was a holy sword.

“Teacher, want to secretly research this together?”

I did not allow him to call me “master,” but in private, when no one else was present, I allowed that form of address—and he showed me the holy sword as he whispered.

I praised him: in these few months, he had learned what a sorcerer truly was.

Because the Church guarded it with strict secrecy, I could glimpse a forbidden realm no one else could know.

Has my life ever offered a temptation so irresistible?

In the end, I could not resist.

No matter how I struggled, I was a sorcerer to the core.

In exchange, I agreed to hear a single “request” of his… but if it was his request, I supposed I could break a bit of a sweat.

Or so I thought—yet the research results on the holy sword were disappointing.

No—let me correct that.

I learned nothing.

Which meant only one thing: I couldn’t even tell whether the sword caused its phenomena through magical power at all.

(Is it truly this unfathomable!? With my knowledge, I cannot even begin to measure it!)

What unknown depths—what fortune.

I trembled at the fact that I alone was peering into a realm no one could reach.

No, in the past, surely other seekers had witnessed this sight.

Even so, in the modern age, the only seeker standing before this abyss—this crystallization of human history’s technology—was me, and only me.

Now I could understand why the Church treated the holy sword as a sacred relic, a supernatural weapon born of the divine.

It was not sorcery.

Nor was it an artifact of the Empire said to have once flourished.

My days with something forged from a system of technology completely unknown to those of us alive now were fulfilling—despite producing not a single concrete result.

But that daily life collapsed sooner than I expected.

“Monster extermination, you say?”

At the marquis’s request, the boy—wielder of the holy sword—was being sent out to hunt a monster.

I felt irritated that my research would be interrupted by such a trifle, but listening closer, it seemed to be a request to subjugate a “new species” that had recently become the subject of rumor.

And the boy had strongly recommended that I be added to the party.

A new species of monster—unknown material.

(An agonizing dilemma. Truly agonizing.)

I never dreamed that at this age I would be blessed with so many opportunities.

Luck I would never have met had I stayed in one place, spending my days polishing and researching.

“Hmm… if I ever have a chance to return home, perhaps I’ll write a book explaining the usefulness of going out into the world?”

I wanted to attach all sorts of conditions to this expedition, but I lacked the standing to do so.

This time, I was an add-on.

So I needed to behave like one, lest I cause needless trouble.

After some coordination with the boy, we ended up saddled with unnecessary baggage and extra front-liners.

He said it couldn’t be helped because he carried the holy sword, but I had no need for dead weight.

I petitioned the marquis to do something about it… and, as expected, nothing changed.

Fortunately—if that’s the right word—the former mercenary with the collar wasn’t bad.

And the man hired on-site was said to have the longest experience operating in the Fullretos Great Forest, so I believed this subjugation would end easily.

But when I finally confirmed the information from the collared ex-mercenary—someone who had actually fought the new species—I heard something unbelievable.

He said: “Normal weapons don’t work at all, it resists magic, its size is ogre-class but its power and speed are on a completely different level.”

On top of that, it had an excellent sense of smell and high detection ability.

And most notably, its intelligence was terrifyingly high.

(From what I hear, it’s troublesome, yes—but only that. Still, what species could mutate into this? It worries me that nothing comes to mind…)

With that impression, I urged the ex-mercenary to continue.

After hearing everything, the conclusion was simple.

“It’s the kind of monster that enjoys fighting.”

An exceedingly rare, abnormal type—one of those cases fit this perfectly.

What they shared in common was “high intelligence” and “extremely high combat power.”

Realizing again that this job was more troublesome than I’d expected, I thought:

If possible, I’d like to call in a specialist hunter (adventurer, now, was it?)—but unfortunately, this was Canaan.

Even if our hand was lacking, we at least had the holy sword.

Part of me felt that would be enough, but in my experience, when you have something reassuring like that, that’s exactly when some unforeseen event trips you up.

I only wish my worry had been needless—but why is it that long years of intuition only hit when you least want them to?

The first encounter was strange enough, but I never expected it to react to that ambush.

“Stronger than anticipated,” I analyzed while remaining hidden.

Even if we couldn’t take its head, I believed it would be a fatal blow—yet when we did it, we only severed its right hand.

And then that hand immediately settled back into place.

(It will need time before it can move perfectly… but that healing is monstrous. Ordinary sorcerers would be useless against this.)

More shocking still: despite that massive body, it avoided close combat and started throwing rubble.

It likely became cautious after losing the hand, but its adaptability could not be ignored.

A powerful monster that learns would become unmanageable.

Before it reaches that point, we must finish it here.

As the new species—Argos—was about to flee, I planted a tracking spell in it.

Losing the guide was painful, but we could not retreat here.

The boy agreed, it seemed, and we chased it by following the magical trail… or so it should have gone.

When the new species appeared again, it held a sword and shield fitting its bulk, clenched firmly—meaning it no longer cared about the earlier injury.

And it recognized magical power.

“The tracking spell backfired,” I clicked my tongue, revealing myself.

Perhaps I had made the wrong call.

But leaving it alive was also impossible.

Since I was joining the second engagement, I meant to settle it there.

Yet when the battle began, it was clear the boy was not at his best, unable to use the holy sword’s power properly.

And though he seemed aware something was wrong, he didn’t understand why; he fought with confusion written all over him.

I wanted to shout, “What are you doing!”—but no one would understand his condition better than he did.

We only needed to buy time and break through with the holy sword… but as the battle turned against us and Alberto began to be pushed back, even that hope wore thin.

In the end, we used the trump card the marquis had given us to retreat.

A crushing defeat, one might say.

But I was not so young as to flee in silence.

I would have my schemes.

The question was how to shake its pursuit… but on that point, the ex-mercenary stated it flatly:

“Impossible. If it wants to, it’ll catch us for sure before we reach town.”

With two young women whose stamina lagged behind, I thought perhaps that was why—but it seemed the thing moved faster than I imagined.

We walked in silence for a while.

At this rate, escaping was all but impossible… so what then?

“Instructor, there’s no time. You will grant my ‘request.’”

The boy said it suddenly.

I understood what he meant whether I wanted to or not.

(It is a role only I can fulfill. No—he believes I can do it. I understand… and perhaps there is no other choice.)

I simply nodded.

That thing was not something we could afford to ignore.

If we did not act now, I could not even imagine what price humanity would pay.

For that, the holy sword—and its wielder—had to make it back, no matter what.

Still, using his “request” here—how merciless.

When I joked, “Show a little more respect for the elderly,” he shot back, “You’ve lived long enough already, right?”

I laughed out loud.

Even if he was a mere youngster, being properly valued made me happy.

Proof that I had lived—surely that man would leave it behind.

Then there was only one thing left: to stake my life and do what must be done.

“Sixty-eight years since birth… more than fifty devoted to the arcane. So this is my grave.”

But I had no intention of dying cheaply.

The honing of my entire life—I may not be able to display it, but you will speak of it, and pass it on.

I left the group behind and walked into the forest.

The killing ground was just ahead.

+++

## **138 - A Certain Mage’s Soliloquy (2)**

The town of Lecorl—

We had made it back, paying in blood, but the other three looked ghastly.

The mage at least understood he’d been dead weight, but the priest assigned as my overseer didn’t even seem aware of it. He hadn’t prepared himself for danger touching him at all, and he was still dragging around his minor injury like it was the end of the world.

And the worst of all was the collared ex-mercenary.

He’d gone as far as openly doubting my ability. The moment we returned, he sneered,

“So is that holy sword just a decoration, or are you simply too weak to use it?”

Honestly, I couldn’t even be bothered to argue back.

Who could have predicted that situation?

No matter what, we had to bring this information home.

That was exactly why—even if it cost a life—we had to get the overseer back safely.

My steps toward the lord’s manor felt heavy.

His deed had to be recorded properly.

That was the least I could offer as a tribute.

“…So you come crawling back with your tail between your legs.”

“Yes. We obtained information that cannot be ignored under any circumstances, so we returned to report and to establish countermeasures.”

In a room inside the manor, with the overseer standing behind me, a stern, middle-aged man seated across from me spoke first.

After I gave him a brief outline, the steward—an administrator entrusted with this matter in the lord’s absence—rendered a judgment without choosing his words, and I denied it plainly, though gently.

“The information we secured, even at the cost of a life, is too significant. That is why a higher decision is required.”

“So you’re saying you want help because you think you can’t handle it yourselves?”

“That’s part of it.”

I nodded without hesitation at the steward’s snide tone.

Behind me, the priest let out a small sound, but I didn’t want him interrupting, so I continued.

“If you call it a lack of strength, then yes—I have no choice but to admit it. However, to avoid any misunderstanding, I will state this clearly… If I cannot do it, then no one can.”

When I asserted that there was no one better suited than me, the steward snorted and spoke without hiding his exasperation.

“People these days love to talk big. Fine—tell me what that confidence is based on. Frankly, I think someone at your level is replaceable.”

“The new species of monster named Argos… that thing has either fled from the elves, or it has beaten them back.”

If I reacted to his barbs, this would go nowhere.

So I tried to start with only the essentials, but the steward laughed under his breath like I’d said something ridiculous.

“Elves, of all things.”

I hadn’t expected him to believe me, but the blatant dismissal still made me want to sigh.

Of course, that was precisely why I’d brought the overseer here.

“Shurin, tell him in your own words. Our opening move should have been perfect. Then why was that monster able to react to me right before this holy sword severed its neck? Why did Argos respond as if it already understood the holy sword’s power?”

The priest—suddenly put on the spot—flustered.

But then, as if steeling himself, he clasped his hands at his chest as though praying, closed his eyes, and spoke.

“The holy sword grants power beyond what a human should possess. That is why the Church has concealed it for so long. Yet the new monster we encountered moved from start to finish as though it clearly understood that threat. There is no other explanation—it must have previously fought someone who wielded a holy sword.”

He said exactly what I needed far better than I’d expected.

He was hopeless in a fight, but there was a reason he’d been appointed an overseer.

“It knew the presence of the holy sword. That explains the impossible responsiveness I mentioned earlier. As a result, our entire tactical plan was rendered useless. To be honest, far too many things happened this time that were beyond expectation.”

“And you want me to believe that? If I’m being blunt, it sounds like you and the Church are inventing an excuse for why you fled.”

“So you’re saying you doubt my ability?”

“You may interpret it that way.”

We were technically dispatched from a marquis’s house… but it seemed the local lord had grievances about this forced assignment.

I didn’t know exactly what relationship existed between this lord and the marquis, but with the Church involved, they couldn’t take too hard a line.

(And yet he’s being this openly hostile… Does he have something he’s willing to risk even souring relations with the Church for?)

Still, regardless of local politics, there were things that had to be said.

“The new species—Argos—without question has experience fighting a holy sword. And its opponent was the elven hero from the Great War. A holy-sword bearer, praised as a sword saint, who cut down the armies of the Great Empire again and again, and was even called the ‘Sword Demon’—a legend.”

“Absurd. That’s a story from over two hundred years ago. Even if he were alive, there’s no reason to think his strength would be unchanged.”

The steward scoffed—yet his complexion had clearly worsened.

“At the very least, there has been no report of the holy sword changing hands. In that case, we have no choice but to consider it. Even I do not have the arrogance to place myself on the same level as that sword saint. And I have no intention of charging in without a plan against something that appears to have escaped from—perhaps even defeated—that legendary wielder. That is why I prioritized returning.”

A living legend with no confirmed death—an individual said to have annihilated an imperial army alone—once that comparison was raised, the steward’s posture stayed stiff, but his thoughts clearly shifted away from me and into grim calculation.

If the records were correct, Canaan had one chance to witness that strength—just before I was born.

Even his earlier line sounded as if he were trying to convince himself.

Perhaps he had been involved in that incident.

“Finally, regarding Argos’s armaments.”

This was the other main point.

I regretted pressing so hard, but he had to understand how serious it was.

Yet the steward dismissed it lightly. “It probably picked them up in some Old Empire ruin.”

“If only that were true… The worst case is if it isn’t. Consider this: why would the Old Empire forge weapons for a monster of that size? The Empire had no reason to produce close-combat weapons like those. And they’re far too refined to assume a monster made them.”

“…I see. So that’s why you say elves.”

“Yes. I’m glad you understand why we had no choice but to return, assuming the worst.”

He really was sharp—he grasped my intent quickly.

“Impossible. Why would they make a move against us? Those shut-ins—why would they attack now?”

But as if to say that understanding and acceptance were separate matters, he rejected my conclusion.

Some speculation was unavoidable.

All our information came from a monster that couldn’t speak with us.

“Still, it isn’t impossible. And if the source is the elves, then many details start to make sense.”

“And that is precisely why it’s the most dangerous scenario.”

I added, then exhaled and looked up at the ceiling.

I waited in silence.

He was surely imagining the worst.

“…I’ll connect you to Lord Kyrias. Wait.”

Next would be a one-on-one with the lord himself.

How much fear I could instill—that would decide everything.

Leaving the manor, I let out a long breath.

The hardest part was over.

Not completely as I’d wanted, but one way or another, we could repay the old mage.

(The problem is that collared man…)

I did have material to persuade him.

The question was whether he’d accept it—and that worry proved correct.

“You really expect me to believe that?”

As expected.

In a rented room at our inn, I told him what happened at the manor.

But the moment the holy sword came up, his attitude shifted completely.

“We didn’t know each other long, but it’s a fact we survived thanks to that old man staking his life. So I decided I’d stick around by my own will until I repaid that debt. But you’re telling me that?”

Honestly, I knew even as I said it that it sounded unbelievable.

But it was the only explanation that fit, so I had no choice but to tell it as it was.

“Fine. I’ll grant you the possibility elves are involved—hard to swallow, but fine. And to confirm whether there’s really a plot, you propose we approach the elves about jointly subjugating Argos. I don’t know a thing about nations’ schemes, so sure—that part I can accept.”

Arms crossed, eyes closed, Orlando nodded as he carefully replayed each point in his head.

“But this—‘you couldn’t use the holy sword properly because its power was being obstructed’—I don’t buy it. I’ll be blunt. If you’re going to make excuses, if you’re going to lie, couldn’t you come up with something less stupid? It’s like you’re making a fool of me.”

He leaned back hard in his chair, furious.

“Yeah, fair. I know. Even I know this sounds like something nobody would believe. But if I don’t think that way… then what happened makes no sense.”

I gestured helplessly, trying to get him to understand how dire things were—but he wasn’t the type to be swayed by hand-waving.

“Hey—do you know the fairy tale called the ‘Glutton Chimera’?”

The ex-mercenary frowned like my topic had suddenly derailed.

“Is now really the time for that?”

“Do you know it?”

Pressed again, he spat,

“No.”

It was a fairly famous story, but mercenaries didn’t exactly live the kind of life that involved reading books. I shook my head.

“Simple version: long ago, a chimera was born from an experiment. It had the ability to claim the abilities of whatever it ate. They fed it all kinds of creatures, and it devoured them.”

Perhaps the content made the danger click, because his mouth hung open as he listened.

“After it gained countless abilities, it became something humans could no longer control. It eventually bit through the chains that bound it and became free—and then it turned its fangs on people. It swallowed whole towns. And in the end, crushed under its own bloated body, it collapsed and destroyed itself.”

The end—and I clapped lightly.

The ex-mercenary stayed with his mouth half open.

“In that story, the glutton chimera is said to have been ‘made by humans.’ But didn’t you ever wonder? Was it really something humans made?”

“…What are you getting at?”

“You—no, you in particular should be able to see it. You fought that new species three times. That’s why I want to ask you.”

-How much stronger had that monster become compared to when you first met it?

At my words, he breathed out only, “Ah…” and looked up at the ceiling.

“It starts to fit too well. If that monster fought elves, if it faced that sword saint… and if it was the kind of creature that kept evolving every time it experienced battle… then everything is explained. It all makes sense.”

That ordinary weapons couldn’t hurt it was conceivable.

But magic barely working, and the holy sword’s power being hindered—that was different.

Unless we thought this way, it would mean the creature had been born with an ability that could respond to a power we haven’t even fully understood.

“Did we… make it stronger?”

“Maybe. Maybe not. Actually… this was the first time I brought up the holy sword at all. Partly because there were too many uncertain factors. But also because I couldn’t shake the fear that we might be creating a new fairy tale right here.”

He stared straight at me.

“It’s a ridiculous idea. That’s why I wanted to confirm it with you first. Then I’ll meet the lord again and speak to him. For that, I need your opinion.”

His answer was silence.

And that silence turned one suspicion into certainty.

He was hiding something.

“I want to make Teacher—Mage Kyrias’s death mean as much as possible. Please help me.”

I bowed my head.

And I regretted it almost immediately.

There are countless things in this world you are better off not knowing.

This was one of the clearest examples.

“…Fine. I’ll talk. About the information on that new species… the truth I—no, we—have kept hidden.”

+++

# **139**

I pushed forward, parting the dense forest as I went.

Two days had already passed since I abandoned the attempt to seize the Spirit Sword and began moving to prepare for winter—by now, I had reached the southwestern edge of the former Imperial territory.

This was my first time setting foot in this area, but just like when I had traveled south before, the dense trees repeatedly slowed my progress.

“I can tell the ecosystem is similar to the south… but the density here seems a bit worse?”

Grumbling that the humidity felt subtly higher, I looked up at the blue sky peeking through the canopy and felt relieved that the weather likely wouldn’t turn bad today.

I carved a path, detouring when necessary, and continued pushing southwest.

At this rate, the degree of natural erosion was probably severe.

I let out a heavy sigh, thinking it unlikely I’d find any intact structures.

Just then, I spotted a space where no trees were growing and found myself drawn toward it.

“Ah—was this an old parking lot or something?”

Though covered in vegetation, fragments of collapsed roofing and other remnants barely preserved traces of what it once had been.

Judging it a good place for a short rest, I halted my movement and began checking the surroundings.

If it hadn’t been completely destroyed by plants, I wondered whether it might not simply be paved ground.

Needless to say, the result was nothing.

It seemed to be nothing more than coincidence.

I had briefly hoped there might have been a military facility nearby, but all I learned was that it had once been a fairly large parking lot, and since this was likely the central area, greening had progressed more slowly.

The absence of even a single vehicle suggested the owners had fled with them.

At present, no idea came to mind for making practical use of this place.

“Remembering that a place like this exists is enough,” I decided, and resumed moving.

Not long after, I discovered a gigantic structure swallowed by greenery.

I found myself staring up at it with my mouth agape—if my memory was correct, this ruin was the entrance to the largest integrated complex ever built outside the Imperial Capital.

That conclusion was based on the massive alphabet letters that still partially retained their original shape, and I couldn’t recall anything else that would fit.

Marketed with the slogan “Everything you want, all in one place,” the multifunctional service area Alma was a theme park funded by numerous corporations and boasted well over a hundred shops.

(Well, as someone who lived in the Imperial Capital, it had nothing to do with me.)

Despite the bluntness of that thought, this discovery brought me a single ray of hope.

Alma, built around the concept of “having everything,” wasn’t just about stores.

In addition to shopping, it offered cinemas, sports facilities, and various leisure attractions, and its outer perimeter was lined with multiple hotels.

Although it failed to become a successful tourist destination, I’d heard its revenue had never been particularly bad.

That meant vast amounts of supplies had once been transported here, and there was a decent chance that something still usable remained.

The Imperial Capital had been blown away, but this was the next largest facility.

There was no reason not to be hopeful.

The underground shopping district—mocked as a “labyrinth”—was especially reassuring as a candidate for a new base.

“I came this far looking for hot springs… but maybe I should prioritize this instead.”

Though the distance was considerable, once a path was carved out, round trips to my usual observation point would become realistic—another big plus.

If I used materials from here to establish something at an intermediate point, even daily travel might not be impossible.

Once that idea occurred to me, my actions were swift.

I parted the greenery and advanced.

This would be my new battlefield.

I felt a faint anxiety about how heavily plants had encroached, but the wall surrounding the theme park seemed to have done its job fairly well—the interior wasn’t as bad as expected.

Which meant they were here.

(The south… orcs, right?)

From today onward, this place would be inherited by me, a legitimate successor and an Imperial citizen.

Anyone without permission would be driven out by force.

However, despite my resolve, I couldn’t find any goblins or orcs.

No—rather than that, I couldn’t find any living ones.

After surveying the area, the possibility emerged that this land was currently a battleground where goblins and orcs were fighting over territory.

(I see—that’s why some areas look like the vegetation’s been cut down.)

That’s when I realized something.

I had entered the theme park through the east gate.

My current position was south of that—far from the center—meaning goblins advancing from the north had pushed this far.

In other words, in the “goblin versus orc” conflict here, the goblins were winning.

“Since the Emperor died, the orcs scattered everywhere—you’d think they’d be the ones with the advantage…”

“So what on earth is going on here?”

Even if the Furon Council’s campaign was going unusually well, this place was simply too far removed.

The presence or absence of a command individual mattered, of course—but if there were genetically enhanced soldiers who once commanded the orcs, that possibility couldn’t be ignored.

“…I should investigate.”

If that were the case, I wanted to know whether friendly contact was possible.

They weren’t mindless beasts that attacked and devoured without thought.

If information could be exchanged or shared, failing to do so would risk future regret.

That was assuming the other side was willing, of course—but as long as neither side knew anything yet, the possibility remained.

With that decided, I added stealth to my exploration.

Activating my camouflage ability, I blended into my surroundings and changed course northward.

And there they were—plenty of them. Those green things.

They treated captured orcs like spectacles, binding them and using them as targets for amusement.

At the very least, it was clear this was indeed a land of conflict between goblins and orcs.

Fortunately, there were no signs that humans had been brought here, and given their numbers, the presence of a queen was certain. On top of that, their oddly coordinated behavior suggested the existence of some kind of irregular factor.

I continued deeper and deeper, advancing quietly while observing.

First, I wanted to identify the queen’s location—but whether she was above ground or underground would drastically affect how long that took.

If I could find her within the time limit of my camouflage ability, all would be well… but if not, deciding whether to withdraw would become a difficult choice.

+++

# **140**

The stench of blood was so thick it made my throat burn.

Amid a scene worthy of being called a “sea of blood,” the lone surviving goblin—the goblin king—rubbed his head against the ground as if begging for his life.

Occasionally, sounds like sobbing escaped him, but in the end, their language was nothing more than animal noise.

Crying, bowing his head, begging for mercy, then attacking the moment you turned your back—at this point, it was practically a template opening scene from cheap erotic comics.

Whether that trope existed because something like this had actually happened, unfortunately, I had no idea.

So I swiftly took the saber in my hand and lopped off the goblin king’s head.

In the end, despite trying to stay stealthy, I was exposed by my scent and a brawl broke out.

Before I knew it, I had reached the theater stage, and that was how I ended up beheading the king here.

I’d probably exterminated around five thousand goblins by now… but that was only the surface level.

And the troublesome part was that it was certain I hadn’t eliminated all command individuals yet. The conclusion drawn from that was simple: this massive horde had been in the process of forming a state.

“Can goblins even make a country?” was a natural question, but records showed that such a case had existed in the past.

The fairy tale of the “Greedy Goblin” was exactly that.

To summarize it simply: “A greedy goblin who wanted everything kept stealing from others, eventually leading a massive horde to seize a country. But that unstoppable advance came to an end through the betrayal of his own subordinates.” In other words, a very typical story.

It seemed to be a fairy tale based on historical fact, and some argued that even before attempting to seize a country, the goblins had already taken on the form of a state—making it eerily similar to the current situation.

“Strict rankings within subdivided groups… I’d assume the underground ones are lower, but just how many are left?”

According to Imperial research, this phenomenon occurred when goblins held multiple kings within a single group. I couldn’t remember the term for it, but the result was easy to describe: the formation of a stratified society.

What began as a pack with a single boss at the top transformed into an orderly group with a clearly defined class system.

Setting aside the question of what “goblin order” even meant, just as barbarians had their own laws, once a command structure was established, they gradually began moving like an army.

That much fell within my knowledge.

What kind of outcome it ultimately produced, unfortunately, I didn’t know.

Still, looking at history, it was obvious that nothing good came from brutal creatures like goblins increasing their numbers.

Therefore, extermination would continue.

With the loss of their king, the surviving goblins were scrambling toward the underground, making it almost certain that the remaining command individual was down there.

(The problem is whether this body of mine can even fit underground.)

I’d originally planned to use this place as a base, but with goblins everywhere, making it usable would be a huge undertaking.

On top of that, it was questionable whether I could completely wipe out those fleeing underground. To put it simply, it was like asking, “Who would want to live in a room infested with cockroaches?”

And to add to that, I still had orcs left to deal with.

The theme park was enormous.

It didn’t take long to decide it wasn’t worth the effort, and I gave up on turning this place into a base.

That said, I had no intention of giving up on the supplies left behind.

I wouldn’t know how badly things had been looted until I checked, but given the sheer size of the theme park, hoping for something usable was only natural.

So while searching for underground entrances I could fit into, I began checking the aboveground shops and warehouses.

As expected, almost everything had been ransacked, and I thought nothing intact remained—but some places with shutters pulled down had been spared.

While casually dealing with goblins gathering toward the underground and wandering around in search of intact warehouses, I realized the sun was already setting.

An all-nighter was guaranteed.

My current haul consisted of exactly one item: a pot.

It was larger and sturdier than the one I was using now—a surprisingly good find.

It wasn’t brand-new, but it had been one of the items stored carefully, so it would probably last a long time.

Now then, I’d found an underground entrance by following goblin traces, but… as expected, the height clearance was a bit questionable for this huge body.

The width was more than sufficient, but the doorway was under two meters tall, making bipedal movement inside difficult, to say the least.

Using both hands to move wasn’t new to me, so that wasn’t an issue, but having trouble wielding weapons was a bit of a problem.

Considering I’d prepared myself for even lower ceilings, this wasn’t the worst-case scenario.

Still, it was undeniably true that I wouldn’t be able to fight at full strength down here.

I steeled myself and entered the underground shopping district.

Naturally, there was no electricity, so it was pitch-dark.

Everything reeked of goblins, making scent-based searching hopeless, but with eyes that worked even in darkness and these ears of mine, finding a large command individual should have been easy.

Or so I thought…

True to its reputation of being mocked as a “labyrinth,” the complex underground layout made it hard to reach my destination.

No—let me be honest.

“…I’m lost. Where’s the guide map?”

Seriously, this wasn’t the Imperial Capital’s underground district—couldn’t they have made it a bit simpler?

Grumbling, I crawled along the low-ceilinged corridors.

Seeing my reflection in a mirror mounted on the wall, I muttered one thing.

“If I weren’t carrying gear, this’d be a full-on horror game.”

A monster crawling through a pitch-black underground mall using all four limbs.

If the victims weren’t goblins, it would’ve been perfect.

(This is probably the type where survivors explore to find mechanisms or keys to escape underground. I feel like there was a versus-style game with a similar setup…)

I’d never played it, but I was pretty sure I’d read about it in a game magazine.

Trying to recall where the safety areas were supposed to be, I wandered up and down aimlessly.

Thinking about unnecessary things while moving was a bad idea, but at least I managed to find a guide board this way.

Aside from the badly deteriorated text and maps, there was no issue.

“Okay… so this is my current position? And the large area where the queen might be is…”

Muttering to myself, I pointed at the board and checked each detail.

Store names were listed, but they were mostly meaningless now.

Still, I managed to determine my position and orientation, and I marked a likely spot where the target might be.

Heading there without detours, goblins began attacking me head-on.

Rejoicing at having picked the right path, I exterminated every goblin that came at me.

No matter how many there were, fodder was still fodder.

Breaking through their defenses with ease, I sliced the queen—a lump of flesh enthroned atop the event stage—clean in two.

I thought, “That’s the end,” but goblins that had been hiding suddenly started running en masse in the same direction.

Watching their retreating backs in stunned silence, I muttered weakly.

“Ah… figures there’s still more.”

An all-night extermination was now confirmed.

+++

# **141**

“…Well, I guess that happens sometimes.”

In the underground district beneath the massive theme park—I broke through a decayed floor and reached the second basement level, then pulled my head back down through the hole I’d punched in the ceiling and muttered to myself.

I brushed debris off my shoulders and head and scanned the area.

Being able to tell what was around me even in pitch darkness made this set of specs feel downright unfair.

(Just occurred to me—technically, I’m classified as a weapon. So what was the concept? With stealth-type abilities and this eyesight, was I meant for infiltration? With this body?)

The punchline of “we didn’t think that far” felt disturbingly plausible for my homeland.

Or maybe the correct answer was simply, “We stuffed everything in,” but… if that was the case, then I didn’t even want to imagine how strong the genetically enhanced soldiers made after me must be.

For now, I wanted to get back up to the floor above, but the moment I grabbed the edge of the hole, the upper floor crumbled to pieces.

“Jumping up is a bad idea, huh.”

Honestly, all I could see was a future where I broke through the floor again.

So I had no choice but to lower my posture and plod forward.

This place had once been lined with restaurants—now it was an abandoned, dim, dust-choked goblin den.

Everything smelled like goblins, so relying on my memory of the guide board from earlier made for an uncomfortably uncertain outlook.

That said, thanks to that information, I made it straight to an escalator, so I won’t complain.

Just as I was thinking I’d go back up to the first floor, I heard yapping voices from below.

They were probably calling out a warning after spotting me, but the fact that it gave them away said everything about their intelligence.

I changed course from upward to downward and headed to eliminate the next command individual.

About twenty goblins blocked my way, but since they didn’t even qualify as stalling or buying time, I’ll skip the details.

I arrived at what used to be a fountain plaza on the third basement level.

Water had stopped flowing ages ago, and the queen sat there arrogantly after having them haul in junk to make her a bed.

Down here, ventilation was poor, so the stench was unbearable.
