I threw a conveniently ripped-off chunk of exterior wall at the screeching lump of meat and completed the mission.

And then the goblins that had been blocking me all ran away in the same direction at once.

“…Still more?”

Given the scale of this place, it wouldn’t be strange if there were plenty left, but despite that, the number of ordinary goblins felt surprisingly low.

Meaning the turf war with the orcs must have been that intense.

When I glanced at a hanging ceiling clock that caught my eye, it was exactly 7 p.m., and I muttered, “So this is what overtime is,” before following the goblins’ trail.

“No—wait!”

I spun around and stared hard at the clock in the fountain plaza.

The second hand ticked along with a faint clicking sound—there was only one thing that could mean.

“The power generation facility is still alive.”

And that wasn’t the only shocking part.

A clock that still kept running even after two hundred years.

I had no idea how far off it was from the real time, but it was an achievement either way.

“Typical of my homeland,” I praised inwardly, nodding in satisfaction as I watched the moving second hand.

At the same time, a sudden question surfaced.

(Two hundred years… and yet it looks way too clean.)

It wasn’t like goblins were cleaning it.

Tilting my head, I kept staring at the clock.

It was a completely casual action.

I shifted my awareness and looked at mana.

And I definitely saw lingering traces—meaning some kind of magical measure had been applied to this clock.

“…What?”

A reflexive voice slipped out, as if questioning the conclusion my own mind had reached.

“No, it’s possible someone from another country happened to come through and cast magic on it. Or a mutant-born Imperial could’ve done it. It’s too early to conclude anything.”

And there was also the obvious question: why a clock?

There was no light, but I moved closer and examined it carefully—visually, it looked completely ordinary.

Yet when I tried to sense mana, it felt like there was something faintly present inside the clock.

I considered opening it, but if I tried to do it with these hands, it was obvious I’d just destroy it.

(But thinking calmly, it doesn’t make sense for it to run for two hundred years without maintenance.)

If I said that, then it was also absurd that I’d been asleep for two hundred years.

Still, there was a convenient phrase that neatly packaged and dismissed all that unnaturalness.

“…Did the Empire use magic in some form?”

A truly “magical” phrase.

But even that simplistic idea hit a snag.

(Rather than concluding they used “magic” directly, it’s more natural to think they scientifically analyzed mana—or succeeded in recreating it somehow—and used it in certain critical facilities…)

Thinking that way made it surprisingly easy to accept.

Limited usage could be explained by cost, and even if it was still experimental, that would also make sense.

Given how abnormally low both the spread and awareness seemed to be, either could be true… but that wasn’t the important part right now.

“Is there any way to get my hands on that technology?” I murmured, glaring at the clock while resting a hand on my chin.

I’d been close to giving up on making this place a new base, but depending on the situation, it might become worth the effort after all.

A plan began forming in my head—and the first thing I’d need to carry it out was?

“In the end, it’s still about eliminating other species.”

I started walking to resume extermination.

Thanks to the time I’d wasted, it took some effort to pick up the escaped goblins’ trail, but I successfully disposed of the fourth command individual, and this time, I confirmed the green things fleeing in all directions.

I pursued them just enough to thin their numbers and plant fear deep enough that they’d stop approaching this area.

If communication within the same species was possible—as it should be—then regrouping with other packs should produce some effect.

With my immediate objective achieved, I returned to the surface for now.

Outside was pitch-black, but compared to underground, at least there was moonlight, so it was better.

(More importantly—what I’m looking for is…)

I slowly scanned the surroundings, but there was no sign of anything like lighting anywhere.

If there was electricity, maybe—was what I’d hoped, but it wasn’t going to be that easy.

Going south to hunt orcs under cover of darkness wouldn’t be a bad option, but first, to uncover the secret of this theme park, I should look for the central management center.

If things went well, I could secure a facility with power.

And if that also meant using various electrical appliances wasn’t just a dream anymore, then it was absolutely worth taking this detour even if it meant forgetting my original goal.

“And of course, I want to rely on a guide board again, but…”

With aging and nature’s encroachment… even if I found one, the question was whether it would be readable at all.

Still, I had no choice but to search—because that’s how the world worked.

The moon hanging in the night sky hadn’t changed from then to now, so why did the world differ so much from my memories?

+++

# **142**

Unpleasant predictions often had a way of coming true.

Maybe it was just that those cases stayed in memory more vividly, but as I looked at a guide board so degraded I couldn’t even tell what was where anymore, my shoulders slumped.

Instead of simply accepting, “Well, that’s how it is,” I shook the metal board violently in a fit of irritation.

Still, it wasn’t a total loss.

From the faint traces of remaining color, I inferred the direction of an arrow and a few letters.

For now, I decided to head toward the general center and gather information there.

Fortunately, a building that looked like it fit the bill was easy to find.

Even inside a gigantic theme park surrounded by walls, any structure left unmanaged for years and exposed to wind and rain would crumble.

With some buildings collapsed, visibility improved, making the target easier to spot.

The problem was that the general center—something that should have been obvious even from a distance—had collapsed.

A building that greeted me with supports bent under weight and smashed into the ground was, in a word, a mountain of rubble.

“Yeah… it’s collapsed.”

Maybe it was luck in misfortune, but as I cleared rubble here and there and searched for anything useful, I found a guide board in surprisingly good condition.

I had to lie flat and shove my face between debris, but I learned the location of the central management center.

At the same time, I also got a rough picture of the park’s overall layout.

I couldn’t make out the southern side, but since I could mostly read the northern side, I could at least guess what might be in the unreadable areas.

I trudged along a ruined asphalt road.

As I walked, I kept scanning the area, searching for anything else that might have been given some sort of magical treatment besides that clock.

Naturally, I found nothing and arrived at my destination.

It went without saying that the building was made for humans, so there was no entrance anywhere that I could fit through.

Even if it was in relatively decent shape, forcing an entry point for a body this huge would definitely risk collapse.

So I carefully searched around the building, and from a half-rotted signboard, I learned there was a loading entrance connected to this place a short distance away.

I couldn’t determine the exact location, so I wandered around a bit—and found a likely spot far more easily than expected.

It must have been a sturdy shutter, because goblins couldn’t do anything to it. I studied the box-shaped structure, still very much intact.

“One entrance. It isn’t connected to the center aboveground, so it must link underground…”

I reminded myself not to get buried alive, then placed my hands on the shutter.

A place that other species hadn’t breached was precious.

So I lifted the shutter carefully, trying not to break it if at all possible.

Depending on its condition, even the base plan I’d nearly abandoned could become realistic again, so I adjusted my strength with genuine care.

Thanks to that, the shutter safely… deformed, and with the bonus of my handprints, it retired from duty as a door.

(It looked fine, but it was rusted and wouldn’t lift cleanly!)

Making excuses to myself, I bid farewell to the barrier that had kept goblins out and stepped inside.

There was no untouched base candidate waiting for me—only the miserable remains of a warehouse that had been thoroughly ransacked.

“Ah… right. This connects to the other side.”

What caught my eye were smashed machines and empty boxes that had clearly been carried off.

Most wooden containers had lost their original shape, but boxes made of metal or synthetic resin hadn’t been broken.

On a whim, I checked the ones in better condition and found that some were still locked and unopened.

I’ll skip the contents since nothing useful came out, but there was one thing I could use.

“Oh… that’s a better fit than I expected.”

I let out a satisfied sound as I looked at the saber nestled into a shallow, upright rectangular case lined with cloth.

I’d been bothered by the blade being exposed this whole time, so it was a welcome solution in an unexpected place.

The edge wasn’t that sharp, so I wasn’t worried about it slicing my gear, but bothersome was still bothersome.

With nothing else standing out, I headed deeper.

And immediately got stuck in a corridor.

Human-sized spaces were narrow and miserable.

So I left my gear behind and tried again.

“Yeah, yeah—you like brute force, right?” I muttered as I twisted my body through the passage like a narrow hole and forced my way through.

I smashed doors along with the surrounding walls and searched the rooms one by one.

I was worried about the ceiling collapsing, but I managed to reach what looked like the right room.

I wriggled into a room packed with monitors, then stretched an arm toward the equipment in front of me in a pose like some middle-aged guy watching TV while lying down on his day off.

I tried operating it, but nothing responded no matter what I did.

“No clue what the problem is.”

Was the power out? Or were the machines broken?

Or was there an issue with the power cables?

Without the knowledge, I couldn’t tell.

With no choice, I went back to searching elsewhere, breaking bits of wall here and there as I moved.

After the date changed and dawn was not far off, I finally found something that looked like a real haul.

I carried it outside, held it with both hands, and spun it around once.

It was a heavy, square safe. After confirming its condition carefully, my chest swelled with hope that it was unopened.

And it had a mana response.

(Meaning either there was something here worth leaving behind even with magical measures… or something they tried to protect even if it required that kind of method.)

If it was documents, it depended on the contents.

If it was a data disc, then it was a dud—I’d have to find functioning equipment to use it.

Low chance, but if it was precious metals, that would be a hit.

“All right, let’s open it,” I thought—and started prying it open… but contrary to expectations, it didn’t budge.

“Got it. It’s reinforced by magic,” I adjusted my assumption and tried again with more strength.

I wrestled with it for about a minute, but even at full power, it wouldn’t open.

“Huh… so bare hands won’t do it.”

Typical of my homeland—this was impressive work, a safe sturdy enough that even I couldn’t break it.

So I pulled out an Imperial tower shield.

Bracing the safe firmly with both feet, I slammed the alloy slab—something even my strongest kick couldn’t destroy—into it with full force.

The metallic ringing made my whole body shudder, but after repeating it several times, the safe finally warped.

After that, I jammed the shield into the gap, widened the opening, and successfully retrieved the contents without damaging them.

And as for what was inside…

To be blunt, it was a miss.

“…Looks like important contracts and paperwork, plus a data disc.”

So much effort for nothing, I thought as I turned the disc over in my hand.

A label reading “Combat Data” was stuck on it, and when I flipped through the papers carefully, I spotted the word “elf,” so I guessed it was from the war era.

I really wanted to know what was on it, but unfortunately I still hadn’t found any intact device capable of playing this data.

Maybe there was one somewhere in this facility, but throughout my search, the only thing that had shown a mana response was that safe.

Idly toying with the data disc between my fingers, I kept flipping through the stack of papers.

Only the soft rustle of turning pages echoed in the looted warehouse.

With fingers that had become a bit more dexterous than before, I pinched a sheet and turned it.

I skimmed it, skipped whatever had nothing to do with me, and moved on.

Soon, what had become routine abruptly stopped—because I thought I’d seen an unexpected term.

I flipped one page back and traced the Imperial-language text with my fingertip.

And I found the word that had caught my eye.

“…The Chimera Project.”

The unexpected find made me mutter aloud.

If that name was here—then what exactly was that “Combat Data” referring to?

To find out, I had to keep searching.

Depending on what was on that disc, it might solve my biggest problem.

But that was only a possibility.

(Still, it’s worth looking.)

First, I’d thoroughly search this place.

But before that, there was one thing I needed to do.

There was still a little time before dawn, but I began reading through the documents I’d taken.

Waiting until it got brighter would be more efficient—but I couldn’t suppress my impatience.

+++

# **143**

In short, there was nothing in these documents that I could call a meaningful haul.

The moment I saw an item labeled “On the deployment of Chimera Project test subjects,” I got my hopes up.

But once I read it, it amounted to nothing more than a roundabout way of saying, “Their appearance is too horrible, so absolutely not,” and there was no real information about the test subjects themselves.

Naturally, there was no information about any facilities involved, either. All that remained were a handful of documents that looked like exchanges with a nearby military base, and it seemed the Chimera Project only appeared in passing.

(In other words, no results. The front line was being pushed back, and there were signs they tried to use this walled place as a defensive strongpoint. They attempted to deploy a Chimera Project product in actual combat here, but it was opposed because it looked too awful. That’s probably what happened… and yet there’s not a scrap of useful detail.)

When I pictured the two test subjects I’d encountered so far, it was easy to accept.

If you imagined something like that rampaging on a battlefield, anyone would reach the same conclusion.

I briefly wondered, “Was that why the Genetically Enhanced Soldier Project was created?” but since I knew how that technology had been converted into military use, I had to reject that quick assumption.

At minimum, that scientist had revealed there were three projects: these two plus the Ultimate Lifeform Project.

Given that documents like this existed, it was likely that around this period they cut their losses on the Chimera Project and advanced to the next stage: the Genetically Enhanced Soldier Project.

“…If I knew how the war situation evolved, I could tell how long Chimera Project test subjects kept being produced. And if genetically enhanced soldiers were being created after that, it would shed light on the period when I became an experiment.”

The problem was that I couldn’t remember how the western front had progressed at all.

If only I’d taken more interest in current affairs—I regretted the student days when I’d done nothing but mess around.

Still, based on the dates in the documents, I confirmed there was a gap of about one year and ten months between when I became a test subject and when the Chimera Project was stalled here.

What I really needed was the time between becoming a genetically enhanced soldier and being put into cryosleep… but there was no way I could know that, and I realized this number didn’t mean much.

(I thought if I knew the period, I could estimate how many test subjects existed…)

I’d consumed two and killed one.

And the other genetically enhanced soldier—known as the Emperor—had consumed two.

“Six, including me.”

That was as far as I could say with certainty right now.

On top of that, there was likely at least one under the Ultimate Lifeform Project.

But the Empire might have already fallen before that, and I also had to consider the possibility that facilities had collapsed—so I couldn’t say “definitely” with confidence.

And facility collapse applied to other test subjects too. The fact that he, who led over ten thousand orcs, couldn’t find a third one also proved it: locating survivors was extremely difficult.

Given the power map—goblins in the north and orcs in the south—it was probably fair to say searching for test subjects in the south was hopeless.

Who knew what might happen—maybe goblins multiplying too much would obstruct the orcs, and somehow that would leave me a chance.

I’d tried not to think about it, but I never expected the day would come when goblins were actually useful.

Still, extermination would continue.

These were done being useful.

Reconfirming that things were far from optimistic, I let go of the documents and began searching for a device that could play the data disc.

No matter what the contents were, I wanted to know.

From the remaining paperwork, I judged that the test subjects had never actually been deployed—but even so, being able to see elven combat power with zero risk would be valuable.

I wished it was a false memory, but among the footage I’d seen on television, there was one where a tank’s armor had been punched through and the crew inside were killed.

Needless to say, that damage wasn’t from a Spirit Sword—and if it was the power of the “magic bow” I’d heard about before, then the arrow aimed at me back then would have gone straight through me.

Fighting forest hunters who possessed multiple such weapons—no, an unknown number of them—would be suicide.

It seemed ineffective against a test subject with extremely strong magic resistance—the one called the Forest’s Nightmare—but I had zero desire to find out how much a lesser version of that resistance could endure in practice.

(Fortunately, using a magic bow is precious—or dangerous—enough that it requires the council’s approval. As long as I don’t take clear hostile action, it shouldn’t turn into the same situation as last time.)

More importantly, the elven martial types seemed to be kept in check by the clan that Number Six belonged to.

Sending silent gratitude to the first thing that came to mind—those impressive breasts—I gathered information to make my safety more certain.

And after searching nonstop until sunrise, I got an unexpected find.

I discovered a point where electricity could be used.

I didn’t know if it was stable, but an outlet I casually tried was live, and a small portable vacuum cleaner I’d picked up during my search reacted faintly.

I hadn’t expected to find this first, but the order didn’t matter much.

Now, if I could find a usable playback device, I’d be able to learn what was on the disc.

Then I kept searching for hours without even eating breakfast.

Around the time the sun climbed near overhead, I finally made progress.

I finished searching every accessible area of the central management center, then checked surrounding facilities—and at the third one, I found it.

“Hmm… ‘Alfort latest-model portable player’—is that right?”

I spoke the inferred name aloud from the letters that remained.

Its storage condition was ideal, and I felt a faint mana response from the device itself.

It was so subtle I wouldn’t notice unless I focused.

When I opened the box, there was a black portable player exactly like the photo.

The manual inside was intact too, and it seemed to be a latest model released after I underwent genetic enhancement.

I flipped through the pages and checked the specs.

For a multifunction model with a list price approaching the upper end of five digits, it was clearly a high-quality product.

Its main purpose was listening to music, but it also had recording functions and radio reception—various unnecessary features that justified being called “multifunction.”

Even so, looking at its overall performance, it was good enough to make me think, “I would’ve wanted this when I was still human,” and as my shoulders slumped, an idea struck me.

“…Should I at least try playing audio?”

Even without a battery, if I plugged in the dedicated cable, it could draw power directly from an outlet.

The accessories for that were included, and if it was high-end, it should support a wide range of data formats.

Muttering that it was worth a try, I returned to the room in the central center where electricity was available.

The moment I turned it on and pressed play, static crackled—and then I heard a faint human voice.

In that cramped room where only my upper body fit, I made a small fist pump.

Then, as the voice gradually became clearer and the noise grew louder, I listened closely—and clearly heard gunfire.

And amid the distorted audio, I distinctly caught the shouts of Imperial soldiers.

“It’s a sniper! Can’t… confirm… him! …got hit… request… reinforcements! Damn it… the tank—!”

“We were… behind the barrier… damn it, this is—! Fall back, before the explosion—!”

A booming blast and rising screams.

More explosions followed in rapid succession, along with broken, intermittent shouting—then suddenly, with a sharp cut, playback stopped.

There was only one conclusion to draw from this audio.

“Wait—seriously… they can punch through a tank with long-range fire?”

+++

# **144**

Information was extremely important.

The more accurate it was, the more reliable my inferences became—and the more it degraded, the more likely I was to make choices based on false premises.

It went without saying what that led to, and in time, people stopped hesitating to spend vast sums on “information.”

Tracing the path of experience others had taken, I listened to the noisy audio data over and over again.

I kept listening to the static-laced recording, searching for anything I might have missed.

And as a result, one suspicion surfaced.

“…This wasn’t a solo operation, was it?”

When it came to elven weapons, the first thing that came to mind was the bow.

In fact, I knew they ran freely through the forest with bows in hand, and it had also been confirmed that they possessed weapons called “magic bows,” which carried pressure similar to a Spirit Sword.

With that in mind, I considered this:

“The gaps are too long if multiple people were firing bows.”

That was what raised the suspicion that the preserved combat record might have been “the work of a lone elf.”

(I remember hearing somewhere that a small group disappearing and wrecking the rear lines made the artillery useless and forced it to withdraw.)

I didn’t think I was underestimating elven combat power—the same combat power that had even overturned overwhelming numerical disadvantages.

But the idea of a single individual striking a unit and inflicting massive casualties was beyond what I’d expected.

It was still only suspicion, but it was more than enough speculation to raise the threat level the Republic posed in my mind.

“…If I could’ve confirmed it with video, I would’ve preferred that, but there’s no point wanting what I don’t have.”

I exhaled deeply and stored the data away on a shelf in the room.

Then I sealed the entrance to this room—where electricity was available—tightly with rubble.

That should prevent anyone from ransacking it.

As long as there was still a chance of playing video, it was probably better to hide it than to carry it around and risk breaking it.

When I went outside, the sun was already slanting, and given how many meals I’d had yesterday, it was about time to hunt for dinner.

With a body that didn’t feel hunger, it was easy to forget, but no matter what shape you were in, you were still a living creature—if you didn’t eat, you’d die.

No matter how advanced Imperial technology was, “fine without food or water” wasn’t possible.

I had endless theories I wanted to work through, but I ran through the forest in search of prey.

The trees were a bit denser on this side, making it a hassle, but I successfully secured three wild birds.

If there were birds, that meant flying stakefish didn’t live around here.

They were still a creature whose habitat range I couldn’t figure out.

And so came “feather-plucking time,” also known as preparing dinner.

By the time my meal was ready, night had fallen completely.

Still, it unquestionably tasted worth the effort, and aside from the small portion, it was a satisfying meal.

There was no bed, but I spent the night in a walled area, and when I woke up, the sun was already high.

I was surprised at how deeply I’d slept, but looking back at everything I’d been doing lately, it wasn’t hard to understand.

This body didn’t feel fatigue easily, but fatigue probably still accumulated without me noticing.

“I really do need to take care of my body,” I reaffirmed, reminding myself not to overtrust this overpowered physique.

This former economic animal no longer had capital besides his own body.

I had to cherish what little I had left.

I didn’t know what I’d had in the first place, but I probably had at least something you could call the result of education.

Anyway, once I was awake, the first thing to do was breakfast.

I went hunting and ate.

After that, I’d spend another day exploring this theme park, then set out again in search of the hot springs that had been my original goal.

I could theoretically make this place a base candidate, but I had absolutely no desire to deal with the huge pile of goblin and orc corpses.

If I caught sight of the grotesque traces of slaughter spread everywhere, the stench alone was enough to make me nauseous.

Clean this up alone? What, was this some kind of punishment game?

For that reason, I had no intention of making this a base right now—but I also couldn’t bring myself to cleanly give up the advantage of having a place where electricity could be used, so here I was again, continuing the search.

Nature’s encroachment was less advanced than I’d expected, making movement easier, and with good visibility, the exploration itself was going smoothly.

However, given the sheer number of goblins and orcs that had occupied the place, there were few intact areas, and human structures were generally too small for me.

“This huge body really isn’t suited for scavenging,” I muttered as I pushed rubble aside and searched for whatever might still exist.

That was when I found something eye-catching inside a collapsed shop.

“For a display, this is ridiculously extravagant.”

A reinforced-glass display case stood out as being in far too good condition compared to its surroundings, suggesting it had been heavily protected.

Inside was a fancy-looking pocket watch—hardly a rare item.

“…A clock that runs for a thousand years, with every component obsessed over and crafted by artisans, huh.”

That was essentially what the description inside the glass said.

As a limited-production item from a famous watchmaker, the price was an outrageous upper six figures—and it even said it was 30% off, which really drove home that the upper class lived in a different world.

All right, then—let’s see if that “usable for a thousand years” claim is true.

I didn’t hesitate and went to solve it with brute force, but the glass was harder than expected.

“Yeah, goblins and orcs wouldn’t manage this,” I nodded, then shattered it with cracking force and picked up the pocket watch.

There was no twist like “it was only a display model,” either. With the premium item in hand, I opened the lid and tried turning the stylish knob on top.

When the second hand started moving, I couldn’t help letting out a small, impressed sound.

“So it really works…”

Imperial craftsmanship was terrifying.

Since there was still a working clock elsewhere, I’d go set this one to match later while I was out exploring.

I didn’t know how much it would be off, but since I was already someone for whom knowing exact time had no real meaning, it didn’t matter.

I continued searching the area where shops lined the paths, and this was what I picked up next:

A small alarm device, a bit smaller than a human fist, with a tiny button. It was broken, of course—but it wasn’t completely silent. It made a limp little hyupon-hyupon sound to “alert” the surroundings that I was in danger.

The sound was fairly loud, too—so even from a bit away, I could still hear it.

Thinking it might work as a security item, I stuffed it into my backpack, and there were no further finds here.

After that, I shifted my search south and finally made contact with the orcs—and then trampled them.

Negotiation or bargaining with these things was a waste of effort.

I’d make them understand exactly who was stronger and crush them so thoroughly they’d never come near this place again.

Facing my brutality—like I was telling them, “From today on, this is my turf. Get out”—the orcs had no answer but to flee.

Driven off like that, they’d head south and end up becoming the Council-State’s problem.

I remembered that orc meat was processed and used for fish farming.

I hoped they’d be hunted down and used to the fullest so it wouldn’t go to waste.

As an aside, goblins and orcs weren’t suitable as meat, and in the Empire they were generally processed into feed for something else. But due to cost, it wasn’t profitable, and because they were such awful pests, they were being exterminated instead.

That said, if they vanished, fiction would just find new ways to use them—so for all how fragile they were, these races were oddly persistent.

I found the ruins of a bookstore while exploring, but there weren’t any books here featuring them.

In a wholesome leisure facility like this, it seemed there was no place for them.

+++

# **145**

As a result of spending almost that entire day exploring, the only things I’d gotten were the following.

A broken alarm

A high-end pocket watch with a small gemstone inside

Sundries

To put it plainly, I hadn’t found anything worth a damn.

If I’d gotten even a shred of useful information, it would’ve been different, but I hadn’t found a single point where power was usable—let alone an intact place that hadn’t already been ransacked.

For the record, “sundries” mostly meant consumables like pens and memo pads.

‘Yeah… too much time had passed. If it had been a hundred years instead of two hundred, there might’ve still been more left behind…’

Whining wouldn’t change anything, so I trudged back along a road littered with orc corpses.

Last, I checked that the lockdown on the room where electricity was still usable was functioning properly, and then I set out.

In the end, by the time I left the theme park, night had completely given way to morning, and I had to secure breakfast—and a place where I could use fire.

The fact that I’d gotten this late because I’d forgotten to adjust the watch’s time, then wandered around because I couldn’t find my way, was downright pathetic.

The current time was 8:30—I closed the pocket watch’s lid carefully and stowed it in my backpack.

As I headed southwest through the forest, thinking about what to do for breakfast, something that caught my eye came into view.

“…Huh? What’s this?”

An artificial object that nature had reclaimed made me blurt it out.

Curious, I checked the thing half-buried in plants and realized it was a vehicle that had likely been abandoned.

I couldn’t tell whether it had been military or not—the original shape was almost completely gone—but the remaining frame was unmistakably that of a car.

Which meant this must have been a road or something like it.

I put a hand to the ground and examined the area for a while… and then I found what I was looking for.

Shattered asphalt—and its fragments.

And the remnants of structures swallowed by nature showed that people had once lived here.

‘From the position and width… a highway? If I’m this unfamiliar with the geography on the west side, it won’t help me pin down where I am.’

For now, I scratched an X-mark into some random structure with my claws so it’d be obvious this was a discovered point.

If I marked unclear points like this in a visible spot, it might come in handy someday.

I didn’t know what it’d be handy for, but if I planted a flag like this, there ought to be a chance it’d connect to something.

As I kept moving west, west, west, the distribution of green I could see through gaps in the trees suddenly changed.

As I approached the mountains, I could gradually feel the slope, and the ecosystem would probably change too before long.

Even through the gaps in the trees I still couldn’t look up and see it, but this was a mountain region with elevations topping 3,000 meters at most.

I didn’t need to go all the way to the summit, but enjoying the view looking down from somewhere along the way wouldn’t be bad either.

Mainly because of the information value—getting a grasp of the terrain.

Who in their right mind would want to stare at endless green wilderness day after day, even if the location changed?

The sun was still high, so I didn’t have to rush, but it was better to have time to spare.

So I picked up my pace more than usual and ran up the nature-rich mountain path—until I finally confirmed a slope that deserved to be called a slope.

Rock faces gradually started to show, and it started to feel like a real mountain.

So that was how I passed the foot of the mountain and found myself in the middle of climbing.

When I looked back, I could see the forest, but I still didn’t have enough altitude to take in a wide view.

I climbed while searching for signs, thinking, “Isn’t there any mountain-like creature around?” but unfortunately, even my nose and ears couldn’t pick up anything.

Either they were too small for me to notice, or there weren’t any… or maybe my sensing ability just couldn’t find them, but there weren’t many wild animals like that.

If anything was here, it was probably monsters.

For the record, how animals and monsters were distinguished differed by country.

For example, in the Empire, any “creature with a structure, ability, or traits that couldn’t be explained scientifically” was defined as a “monster.”

Apparently that led to many creatures being treated differently than in other countries, and I suspected that had been one cause of conflict too.

Between a neighbor who said “Coexisting with monsters is possible” and another neighbor who said “Monsters? Fine—kill them,” there was bound to be friction.

Anyway, with nothing particularly noteworthy, I kept climbing until the slope got a bit harsher.

“Well, I’m not following a proper mountain trail, so this is probably normal,” I said, and looked back.

I looked down at the green that filled the sky and earth without a hint of emotion.

I searched the scenery carefully, wondering if there was any sign of man-made objects—or what remained of them.

The first thing I found was the theme park’s outer wall from where I’d been just a little while ago, and the structures visible beyond it.

“Even from this height, I can still see it,” I muttered, but since I still didn’t have enough altitude, only a tiny portion was visible.

I considered climbing higher, but with this huge body, there was a risk the footing would collapse.

First, I’d survey the surroundings from here—and while I was at it, look for a route to climb.

I moved slowly and carefully, staring down at the forest and squinting for “anything.”

After repeating that for a while as I walked along the mountain slope that was starting to feel punishing, I spotted something that clearly didn’t belong.

And it wasn’t in the forest—it was on the mountain.

(…A marker? It looks pretty new.)

It was a wooden stake driven into the ground.

If it had been just a stick, I could’ve ignored it, but unfortunately there was a skull of some creature hooked on top, and the bone rattled in the wind.

I let out a sigh. “So there are barbarians on this mountain.”

I’d never heard of goblins or orcs having a custom like this.

Which meant it was some other creature—a monster—but unfortunately, nothing in my memory did something this tasteless.

When I got closer, the stake was bigger than I’d expected, and the skull on it was proportionally large too.

I’d heard of birds impaling prey on branches to store it, but there was no way this was a size birds could manage.

Judging from the skull, the owner of this head was likely a creature with a body over two meters tall.

“…For a territorial marker, it’s a bit too violent.”

And for a human to do it, it was primitive to the extreme.

It probably wasn’t something that had formed naturally, but I figured I should confirm it, so I brought my face close to the crude object.

Then, from some distance away, I heard a cry.

Right after that, I sensed the sound of something coming this way.

Looks like the maker of this thing was about to show up. I looked over, but because of the terrain, I couldn’t see them at all.

After waiting there for a bit, the thing appeared in a way that made me look up.

A creature that was incredibly close to humanoid, covered head to toe in fur—yeah, a monkey.

But it was huge.

Absurdly huge.

There was still some distance and I couldn’t see its whole body, so I couldn’t tell its exact size, but I couldn’t help thinking it might be bigger than me.

A monkey of utterly abnormal scale.

“KYIIEEEYAAAAAA!”

The monkey bared its fangs and roared at me.

So what, I scoffed.

Maybe it didn’t like my reaction, because the monkey shut its mouth and leapt down from higher ground.

And then—I saw something impossible.

I knew very well what it held in its hand when it landed.

While I was staring at it, the great ape raised the weapon overhead.

No speed, no technique—yet even that kind of blow still managed to graze my arm, because I was too slow to jump back.

I wiped the faint blood with my thumb and returned my gaze to the huge saber in the ape’s hand.

This wasn’t something I could dismiss as “similar”—the shape, and the material that could actually injure me.

“Hey, monkey. Why don’t you tell me where you got that?”

A special-alloy saber made for genetically enhanced soldiers—one exactly like mine.

Why was some giant ape I’d never seen before carrying it?

There was no verbal reply—only another downward swing of the blade.

+++

# **146**

I yielded the initiative and watched to see what it would do.

I hadn’t meant to, but not switching to a counterattack so I could learn what this great ape was—despite it launching an attack without hesitation even after seeing me—could only be called a mistake.

“Kiiyeeeeyaaaaa!”

I wouldn’t call it a piercing shriek, but watching this ape swing its saber around while screaming the whole time left me with only one thought.

It felt like I’d stumbled onto the scene of a drug addict waving a blade around.

For now, I dodged its slashes and tried calling out things like “Hey,” and “Listen to me,” but it didn’t react.

In that case, it was either just a monster, or a subject who’d already lost all human reason.

If it were the latter, that would’ve been the best outcome in a sense, but with this ape’s combat ability, I had to admit that possibility was unlikely.

“AAAAAAAAGH!”

Maybe it got irritated that no matter how many times it swung, it couldn’t even graze me, because the great ape roared up at the sky.

The terrain was bad, so it was true this was a hard place to fight—but this level of disadvantage didn’t even count as a handicap.

Still, I wasn’t kind enough to go easy on it, so I reached back to the pack on my shoulders and pulled out the case holding my weapon.

Then, carefully, I took the saber out of the box and extended one hand forward as if to point the tip at it.

My other hand still held the case, but against this thing, my right hand alone was more than enough.

When I drew a weapon identical to its own, the ape started to panic.

It panicked so obviously it was almost funny—letting out small growls as it backed up one step, then two.

But instead of choosing the correct option—whether that caution was prudence or cowardice—it let out a screech and lunged at me.

As I slipped sideways past its savage, weighty strike, I cut upward at the same time.

Right on cue, the ape’s arm flew into the air, and the great ape sprayed blood from the severed stump as it shrieked, “Gehii!” and tried to flee.

Of course I wasn’t about to let it, so I brought my saber down from the upswing, aiming for the legs as it turned its back and ran.

Its left ankle was cut, and the moment it fell, it tumbled down the slope.

I caught up with a single leap, stopped the rolling ape with a kick—and drove my saber, held in a reverse grip, into its heart.

It struggled while coughing blood, but with an arm missing from the elbow down, it could do nothing—only staining my body slightly red before it went still.

“I knew it, but… it was too weak.”

I pulled the saber out and headed first to collect the spoils.

I didn’t need it, but getting a spare weapon was extremely welcome.

The problem was that it hadn’t been maintained at all, and I could see chips and warping even with the naked eye.

It looked like it had been used for quite a long time—the saber the great ape carried was in terrible condition.

‘As-is, it’ll be hard to use even as a spare.’

With a sigh, I put my own saber—after wiping off the blood—back into its case, then approached the ape’s corpse with the battered one in hand.

I’d already reached a conclusion, but I still had to confirm it.

I considered cutting off a part, but a severed arm lay nearby on the slope.

So I thought, This’ll do, and sank my teeth into the arm I’d picked up.

The stench of blood and the feel of flesh—there was no change even after I bit through.

“Yeah… figures.”

I spat out the meat mixed with blood and exhaled.

I hadn’t expected anything, so I shouldn’t have felt disappointed, but my mood still grew a little heavy.

So I decided to think about it from another angle.

‘This ape definitely wasn’t a subject. And this saber was definitely made with Imperial technology. So where did it get it?’

Normally, the obvious answer would be: it found it in the ruins of an Imperial facility.

Then what was that “facility”?

If it was related to genetically enhanced soldiers, it had to be either a laboratory or a place where subjects were stored.

“So that means there’s a facility within this ape’s range?”

And I could even imagine who it had been storing.

The one called the “Emperor” had probably come out of a facility somewhere around here.

Which meant that when the entrance opened, something else could get in.

“…This feels a lot more like the right answer than I expected.”

If that was the line I blurted out after reasoning it through, maybe I should trust myself a bit more.

I tended to underestimate myself because the rest of my family was too high-spec, but I should’ve had at least enough ability to be called ordinary.

Then it wouldn’t be strange for me to hit the right answer once in a while.

If anything, judging from the information I already had, it was starting to feel like the difficulty was pretty low.

Either way, with an Imperial genetically enhanced-soldier saber sitting here, it was practically certain there was a facility not too far away.

That information was extremely valuable.

To think I’d come looking for hot springs and ended up with something like this—I could say with confidence that this bold relocation had been the right call.

Now all that remained was how to find the facility… and for that, there was no way around steady, methodical searching.

Luckily, there was still time before the sun set.

While this momentum held, I’d search the surrounding area.

With that in mind, I spent about an hour using my long-range vision to scan the forest and the mountainside.

“I can’t believe it was that easy to find…”

I didn’t know whether it was a research facility, but I’d spotted something that looked like a structure still holding its shape in the forest—more like near the foot of the mountain—and I was heading there now.

Because of the difference in terrain and ecology, it wasn’t ideal for running, so I moved carefully, mindful of my gear.

And when I reached my destination, I couldn’t help muttering,

“…What the hell? How does that make sense?”

The entrance had clearly been camouflaged by human hands.

Staring at an artificial object hidden by an unnatural gathering of “nature,” I tilted my head.

The most plausible answer was that whoever had come out of here had hidden it… but then it didn’t make sense that a great ape carrying that saber existed.

I thought hard for a moment, then realized: I’d learn more by going inside, and started pushing aside the plants covering the entrance.

Then I opened the double doors and slipped inside.

“…Is someone here?”

The interior was so clean it would’ve been strange if no one had been maintaining it.

It looked like the invasive plants had been removed, and the materials of the floor and walls were clearly visible.

Keeping my body low, I moved deeper, searching for a way down underground.

In the process, I realized one thing.

Only a small portion had been cleaned, and areas where the outer walls had already collapsed were left abandoned.

On the other hand, intact sections and certain corridors had been cleared of plants.

‘It’s like only the necessary parts were being cleaned.’

Was someone using this place as a base?

If so, who?

To get my answer, I pressed on.

I found it quickly.

An elevator that likely led underground—one large enough that even subjects of the genetic enhancement project could ride it.

The area in front of the doors had been cleaned spotless, and I reflexively started to press the button to call the car.

As I gave a wry smile at myself—What am I even doing?—my ears caught it clearly.

“…It moves?”

The sound gradually drew closer, then stopped at nearly the same level, and the elevator doors opened.

At that sight, I just stood there blankly, forgetting to step in.

I wedged my hand into the doors as they started to close, and when they opened again, I slipped into the dark, unlit elevator.

Without a word, I pressed the button for what seemed like the lowest level, and when the doors closed, the car began to descend.

Less than a minute later, the doors opened in front of me, and I stepped out, scanning the surroundings.

There were no lights on the ceiling when I looked up.

But I could still make out where things were.

A gate—there was a massive wall in my line of sight that was beginning to move with a grinding sound.

Light started to leak through it, slowly illuminating the dark space.

And as the gate split and opened, a shadow fell into the gap.

“Welcome back. I never thought you’d return he—…Who are you?”

A man in a white coat popped out from the opening gate and asked me suspiciously.

+++

# **147**

As if he couldn’t care less about the obvious question of “Why is there a human in a place like this?”, the man who’d come out tilted his head at me and kept letting out low grunts.

“I’m—”

“Ah—wait, wait! I think I can remember. Hold on a second.”

As the man in a white coat tapped his temple with his index finger, trying to dredge up his memory, I wondered what I should do.

I was stuck between two choices—wait, or ignore him and speak anyway—when he finally seemed to recall something and lifted his face.

“Right. ‘Goliath.’ A successful Type ‘Goliath’ case. There were three, I believe, and one of them went berserk and was disposed of.”

Then that meant I was one of the remaining two, he thought, nodding in satisfaction at having remembered.

“And you—who are you?”

“Your name was… hmm. Me? I’m just a sponsor.”

I tried to make a suspicious face at his words, but with these useless facial muscles, my feelings didn’t get across.

“What does that mean?”

“Exactly what it sounds like. You know the Notos Corporate Federation, right? I’m affiliated with it. I was also a subject of genetic modification technology, and a few years ago I woke from cold sleep, so I’ve been active here ever since.”

A subject of genetic modification technology—that phrase made my body tense for an instant.

I’d thought, What a haul, but the words that followed from his mouth only confused me.

For the record, I’d only ever heard the name Notos Corporate Federation.

“Well, even if I say ‘subject,’ it was original technology. I don’t have a tough body and abilities like yours, and I obviously don’t have a Nemesis Code either. It was used properly for medical purposes, so sorry, but I can’t meet your expectations.”

“…What are you talking about?”

“To put it simply, I had a somewhat special disease… and current medical technology couldn’t cure it. So I tried to do something about it with genetic modification technology I happened to learn about—by forcing it through with my sponsor authority. But then—”

He clapped his hands once and leaned forward as if to peer into me, then straightened back up and resumed his explanation, moving his fingers together as he spoke.

“It turned out it would take far too long to create the final piece needed for a complete cure. That’s where the cryosleep device came in. It’s no exaggeration to say that it only became capable of running for centuries because of me.”

He changed his expression as if joking—You can thank me—then immediately turned serious again and continued.

“In other words: even if you eat me, nothing will change. That’s what I’m saying.”

“How much do you know?”

“Only what I can learn with sponsor authority—meaning I know nothing at the level of state secrets. Sorry, but I doubt I can be much help. More importantly…”

He glanced toward the gate, now fully open, as if to say come inside, and I followed him, walking that way.

Beyond the gate was a space that didn’t feel like it had aged two hundred years at all.

It felt almost like a hospital, and a wave of nostalgia came over me as I looked around.

“Alright—let’s start with introductions. My name is Lark. Lark Adamas.”

When my attention drifted to my surroundings, the man turned back and introduced himself.

“Adamas… that’s a count’s house, isn’t it? How’d you get into the corporate federation?”

“Ah, the idea that nobles and corporations hate each other is just a rumor. If anything, we intentionally spread that kind of talk. If the upper echelons get too close, the annoying types start yelling about collusion. So even moving funds openly takes procedures. My illness was a convenient excuse, but… with results like this.”

Lark took a hands-up gesture. I asked, “So it didn’t cure you?” but he said nothing and started walking.

“…Two hundred years was too long. The drug I was supposed to use at the end had gone bad.”

“That’s… how should I even—”

“Ah, luckily my sense of pain is gone. I have a life that might end tomorrow, but thanks to that, I was able to steel myself.”

Lark answered in a bright voice when I trailed off.

It wasn’t hard to imagine the turmoil it took to reach that point.

But I sensed his will—I won’t disgrace myself as an Imperial noble by wailing and raving—and without thinking, I placed my right hand over my chest and bowed my head.

“…The Empire is gone. There’s no need for that sort of ritual, Yunos-kun.”

I’d meant the gesture as respect, but that response made me lift my face reflexively.

“I just remembered. When I was looking through the roster of subjects, I remember wondering, ‘Why would you, who should’ve lived in the Imperial Capital…?’ It stuck with me.”

“I don’t remember much else,” he added with a self-mocking smile.

“But… your mother and your elder sister were famous in the industry. Normally, because of how far they went, you would’ve been sent to the battlefield. Yet they stopped that from happening without making more enemies. It was impressive work.”

My reaction was flat—“Oh, so that was a thing?”—and Lord Lark tilted his head.

“Well, they stood out no matter what. Especially because there was so much jealousy—people trying every trick to bring them down, and others trying to recruit them outright. Incidentally, I was one of those people. I was actually quite serious about courting your elder sister, but…”

He added, almost accusingly, that my sister was a little too chaste, and I lowered my head with a wry smile.

“Uh… I’m sorry.”

“No, no. There’s no need to apologize. Considering her charm, it’s only natural. There were lots of rivals, sure—but I feel like back then, I was living the youth I never had in my teens.”

Laughing, he sat down on a nearby chair, studied my drastically changed body, then lightly clapped his hands once.

“So—what do you want to ask first?”

It was helpful that he was willing to get to the point from his side.

I could ask without holding anything back.

“What is the Nemesis Code?”

I had a guess as to what it was.

But I wanted to hear a clear answer from someone who knew.

“I’ll skip the fine details since you’ve probably already connected the dots… but you’re right. It’s a self-destruct program secretly planted to prevent military use by Dr. Zeta, the inventor of genetic modification technology—the same researcher who sold that technology to the Empire. That’s the official Nemesis Code.”

“Official?”

Lord Lark nodded at the word that slipped out of me.

“Yes—official. Put simply, the Nemesis Code was never truly a secret. Any researcher involved with genetic modification technology back then knew it existed.”

“…They knew?”

My mind couldn’t immediately grasp what that fact meant.

So he explained.

“Because without dirtying their own hands, it became insurance in case a subject couldn’t be controlled. If they claimed ‘we didn’t know,’ most scandals could be wrapped up as the doctor’s responsibility. The leadership and upper ranks responsible for the Ultimate Lifeform Project that began with the Chimera Project knew the Nemesis Code existed, had a way to stop it, and still decided to continue the experiments as-is.”

At that point, I understood.

The Empire had treated me… treated subjects… not as people, but as weapons.

The fact that we’d once been human wasn’t considered. Disposing of us as weapons had been on the table—and they had, in fact, made that decision.

‘It feels like the gears in my head just slipped out of alignment.’

The Empire I’d imagined and the reality in front of me didn’t match.

I could feel my image of what an Imperial citizen—an Imperial soldier—should be crumbling.

No, I shook my head. I’d just been forcing a convenient ideal onto it.

I’d been conscripted and joined, but it had also been my choice—so I’d listened to a shady offer and chosen to become a subject.

Even if I said something now—no matter who I resented—it probably wasn’t what I truly wanted.

I couldn’t accept it.

But I could understand it, at least.

More than anything, I looked up at the ceiling and swallowed the fact that “the ones who made that decision are already gone.”

Then his words continued.

“…And the one who approved the plan to dump every inconvenient part onto the doctor was me.”

+++

# **148**

“—That was a joke.”

My mind stopped keeping up with the sudden confession, and just as understanding began to sink in and my emotion-suppression function finally started kicking in again, he hit me with that.

The strength drained out of me on the spot. My knees buckled, and an exasperated “Eeeeh… seriously?” slipped out.

“Think about it. There’s no way I could decide how military technology gets used just because I’m one sponsor of a corporate federation, is there? And it wasn’t peacetime—it was wartime. Try refusing to repurpose a technology that can be militarized. You should be able to imagine what would happen.”

He leaned in, peering at my face with concern, as if to say I might be a little too easy to fool.

His tone was so smug it made me want to release the arm I’d just barely restrained—but given what I could already guess about him, doing that might be exactly what he wanted.

“To be precise, I was only one of those who approved it. Even if I’d refused, the outcome probably wouldn’t have changed. And for the record, what I was first told was only something like ‘we’ll take advantage of Dr. Zeta’s interference.’ I doubt anyone imagined it would turn out like that.”

My suspicion that he loved to talk was right—he kept going even when I stayed silent.

Granted, if you lived alone in a place like this, you’d probably want to talk the moment a conversational partner appeared.

He was the first human I’d met from my own era.

I had countless things I wanted to say too.

Even so, I let him talk because I’d realized my remaining time was probably far longer than his.

“—That said, back then I’d just been hit with the reality that my illness might not be curable. If you told me it would hinder research that was originally meant for medicine, then even if I’d known all the details, I probably would’ve approved anyway.”

In the end, even if he’d objected, the plan would have proceeded, and the moment I hadn’t refused the scientist’s offer, this outcome was already set.

Once I understood that, Lark’s words couldn’t shake me anymore.

“Oh, right… If I’d managed to win Mireina over, that would’ve made me your brother-in-law, wouldn’t it?”

He spread his arms wide with an infuriating grin, full of mischief, like You can call me that if you want. I answered by making a fist, and Lark shook his head.

“You really don’t get jokes, do you.”

“More importantly, I’ve got a lot I want to ask.”

Whatever respect I’d had earlier was long gone—my speech wasn’t even polite anymore, and I decided to ask what I wanted to know.

I didn’t need to look to tell he hadn’t talked enough yet.

He accepted my proposal readily, leaning back in a body that couldn’t be called healthy.

“Ask anything you like.”

“First question. About Type Goliath.”

“Huh? You’re asking that?”

He said he didn’t know many details, so I briefly told him about when I’d awakened. Lark nodded.

“I see…”

“That said, I don’t know that much either. If I recall correctly, Type Goliath was a genetically enhanced soldier developed to capture enemy strongholds alone by switching between Stealth Mode and Assault Mode.”

My reaction to learning the concept behind me-as-a-weapon for the first time was a simple, satisfied: “Yeah, that tracks.”

Stealth Mode didn’t even need explaining.

So did that mean my normal state was called Assault Mode?

As I started to feel a little excited—So I might be able to power up?—Lark continued.

“As far as I know, it was the newest model of genetically enhanced soldier, and the best overall in total specs. Back then, the front against the Republic wasn’t going well, so I remember hearing something like, ‘We gave it abilities that let it throw their own tactics right back at them.’”

Hearing that, I assessed my own specs coolly. “Yeah… I can’t say I can’t do that.”

‘So I was the latest model at the time—and top specs, huh…?’

I grinned to myself.

My facial muscles still didn’t work, but I nodded as if to say go on.

“And then… yeah, right. There was something you’d be interested in—about the Ultimate Lifeform Project. Like you know, it was an attempt to create the strongest biological weapon, building on the Chimera Project and the Genetically Enhanced Soldier Project. But as the war situation worsened and time ran out, the engineers decided to develop that biological weapon using genetically enhanced soldiers as the base.”

At that point, I could tell where this was going.

“And the one chosen for that was—”

“No, I don’t know which one was chosen.”

My knees nearly gave out again.

“I went into the sleep device around that time. Sorry, but I don’t know which type became the base.”

He laughed and spread both hands slightly to either side. I gave Lord Lark a dubious look, and he stared up at the ceiling and added,

“But—”

“I do know there were three candidate types. One was, of course, Goliath, like you. Next was Behemoth, specialized even more heavily for combat… and the last one was… what was it again?”

He thought for a while, then threw in the towel.

“Nope. Can’t remember.”

“Well… if I know that much, it’s probably enough.”

Apparently this Type Behemoth was an even bulkier heavyweight version of me—during a demonstration, it had hugged a tank and crushed it flat.

“Yeah, I definitely never want to meet that,” I muttered, and moved on to the next question.

Or rather, this was the main one right now.

“Next question. Do you know where any research facilities exist besides this one?”

“That’s a hard question to answer.”

Lark said it with a wry smile. I stared at him.

“The answer is yes. But I don’t know if it’ll help you. I don’t know whether anyone at the places I know has the Nemesis Code, and I don’t even know if those facilities still exist.”

“All right,” I replied, just that.

Among what I’d expected, it wasn’t a very good answer.

Knowing only rough points where facilities might be didn’t change the fact that finding them from inside this forest would be difficult, and even if I had a detailed map, it would be useless in the current situation.

In other words: I could narrow the range, but had almost no way to pinpoint the location.

Still facing hardships and unable to move forward—given that even the terrain had changed by now, the information wasn’t worthless… but it also wouldn’t dramatically change the situation.

“Oh, right. When I got here, you mistook me for someone, didn’t you?”

“Inos-kun, you mean? He was… Type ‘Ogre,’ I think. A mid-stage prototype of a genetically enhanced soldier that assumed it would be armed. He and I were from neighboring facilities, you see. After we talked about what we needed to, he left—and I haven’t seen him since.”

It was a side question, but it turned out to hold a surprising amount of useful information.

“There’s another facility next to this one? And how much do you know about what’s in the Nemesis Code?”

“Calm down. One question at a time. First—this facility was where Inos-kun was sleeping, and the one next door was where I was. If there’s anything you need, go ahead and take it. Second—about the Nemesis Code, it’s as I said earlier. Most people involved knew it existed. As for the content, only a tiny handful of the people involved knew. If you’re talking details, it was probably only a few people in the upper ranks…”

He then bragged that he was part of that tiny handful.

But he still didn’t seem to know the core details. At best, he only had the by-now unsurprising information that “it was rigged so subjects would kill each other, and by eating the other person, you advance to the next stage.”

I was about to move on to the next question, but the topic drifted and his bragging just kept going.

Still, I didn’t feel like stopping him.

‘He definitely said it—“a life that might end tomorrow.”’

As if proving it, Lark carried the clinging smell of blood.

A chemical stench mixed into it, telling the story of his despair.

There was no way any medicine here would still be intact.

Even so, life wasn’t something you could simply give up on—something I understood myself.

So I kept listening in silence.

As someone in a similar situation, as a compatriot from the same era, I still had enough humanity left to want to do what I could for him.

+++

# **149**

Lark’s “turn” started again, and I ended up having to listen to all sorts of noble-society nonsense I didn’t particularly need, but when it came to the research-facility information I wanted most, it ended with a single line: “I’ve got something I scribbled all over a map back when I was bored.”

He also told me where it was, so even if he suddenly dropped dead in front of me, I’d be fine.

“Oh, right. There’s something important I forgot to say.”

I cut into Lark’s endless chatter with that.

He frowned for a moment, but the moment he heard “important,” he went quiet and waited.

“Turns out I looked into a few things… and while the Empire lost the war back then, it seems it gathered its remaining forces and pushed south, wiped out Reberen and Haile, and survived as a state by renaming itself the Furon Council-State.”

Lark’s eyes widened.

He looked genuinely shocked—mouth half open, staring at me—so I nodded to confirm it was true.

“I see… I thought it had fallen…”

He lowered his face and muttered while thinking, nodding to himself, then looked up at me with a smile.

“That’s good news. Very good news. Thank you.”

His eyes were slightly watery as he nodded again and again, so I tossed in one more piece of news.

“Also, I found a soldier who looks exactly like my sister. I talked to her, and personality-wise, it’s completely my family’s woman.”

For a moment, Lark looked blank, then he laughed and gave me a skeptical look.

“Seriously?”

“She was so similar it was scary. Even her voice sounded the same—I almost called her ‘Sis’ by accident. Oh, and her figure’s close too, but since she’s a soldier, she might be a bit more muscular.”

“That almost makes me want to see her,” he said, sounding genuinely tormented, folding his arms and groaning again.

Then he shut his eyes and fell silent, as if thinking hard.

Until a moment ago he’d been moving his mouth nonstop—now he wasn’t saying a word.

“By the way, you can use electricity here, right? Do you have anything that can play a data disk?”

I asked, but there was no answer.

As I reached toward Lark, who’d stopped moving with his arms folded and head bowed, I noticed something.

The smell of blood—a thin red line slipped from his closed lips, beading at his chin before dripping down.

“Hey—don’t push yourself!”

My voice came out harsher than I meant, but Lark raised a hand to stop me, spat out the blood in his mouth, and took a deep breath.

“No, today I felt unusually good, so I got careless. Don’t worry—coughing up blood happens all the time.”

“…What’s wrong with you? I’ve got potions. Want one?”

I reached into my pack to pull out the blue and green bottles, but Lark shook his head.

“Unfortunately, this isn’t something mass-produced medicine can fix. Even after choosing genetic modification, it only becomes possibly curable if you prepare a dedicated drug. I’m not even sure it would buy time.”

He waved a blood-smeared hand and wiped at himself with his white coat, putting on a brave front.

“I told you earlier—literally, ‘a life that might end tomorrow.’ Honestly, I’m impressed I’ve lasted this long. If I think it was all to meet you and learn what became of the Empire… it almost makes me want to thank God.”

The way he said it—like he’d survived only for this moment—left me with a complicated feeling, and I didn’t know what to say.

“I want to tell you not to make that face… but, well, your expression really doesn’t change, does it?”

Lark joked with a laugh. He probably didn’t like heavy atmospheres.

So I opened my mouth.

“Yeah, but at first, I couldn’t even talk like this, you know? The second I opened my mouth, all that came out was ‘Gaa gaa.’ I had a hell of a time whenever I ran into humans.”

So he wouldn’t overdo it, I started talking instead.

How I’d woken underground and struggled to reach the surface.

How I’d begun surviving in the forest and gradually started getting used to this body.

How I’d run into humans and rescued a naked, big-breasted beauty.

How I’d encountered another subject, fought, and killed them.

As I went through everything in order, the story finally shifted to the elves.

“—And she was perfect, just from looking at her. So I named her Number Six-san and kept watching her as my top-priority observation target.”

And then, at last, Dame Oppai entered the story.

That level of “dame” probably couldn’t be conveyed with words, but he seemed to grasp something anyway, because he cut in during a pause in my breathing.

“How should I put it… you seem to be enjoying that form of yours quite a bit, don’t you?”

I couldn’t exactly deny it.

But I wanted him to understand that it was the result of overcoming countless hardships—I wasn’t just living carefree.

“You look a bit better now, yeah? Then sorry to be blunt, but back to my earlier question. Is there anything left that can play a data disk?”

He made a thinking gesture, but the answer didn’t meet my hopes.

“No… there isn’t. In the first place, normal methods can’t endure two hundred years. If anything does exist, it would be…”

“Magical methods,” I finished.

Seeing Lark nod turned my suspicion into certainty.

“So nobles really do know about it.”

“Of course. You need it to understand your enemy, and there are plenty of situations where you’d be in trouble without it. But a data disk…”

He seemed to guess my circumstances on his own—because I was asking only because I knew the contents were intact.

I felt a little nostalgic. This is exactly how conversations with my family used to go.

“There was a theme park nearby, right? I found a disk there—combat data preserved through magical processing. I want to know what’s on it.”

“Combat data… ah, the ridiculous ‘spirit weapon’ thing?”

“No. The footage is a tank getting pierced by a long-range snipe.”

Apparently there was data for the spirit sword somewhere too, and I assumed he’d mistaken it for that—right until new information stabbed me in the face.

“Hm? You mean the spirit bow, don’t you?”

“…What?”

“Hm?”

A human and a monster stared at each other.

“Wait. What’s a spirit bow?”

“I mean spirit weapons.”

While I clutched my head—There are ones besides a sword!?—he hit me again without mercy.

“Besides the sword, there’s a spear and a bow.”

Reading that I’d only known about the spirit sword, Lark added more information with a pale, amused smile.

And apparently the elves had all of them, because he kept talking like there was more.

“As far as I know, the Empire confirmed five spirit weapons in total. Three swords, plus one spear and one bow. There was one particularly large sword, I think, but that’s just the difference between a one-handed and two-handed sword… there’s no need to split hairs beyond that.”

I’d thought it was four total with what I knew—turns out it was five.

‘So I was getting cocky about being the ultimate lifeform, but there are this many things that can kill me?’

And that was only what had been confirmed.

I’d just concluded I should stop getting carried away, when he dropped another bomb.

“Elves have something called the ‘Three Great Clans.’ Those three clans each possess a sword, a spear, and a bow—and they produce the users for them. In the Empire, the most famous one was Corner, the spirit sword clan, but the others barely surfaced publicly, so surprisingly few people knew them.”

Three Great Clans—that phrase rang a bell.

Right—who had I heard that from again?

“Corner for the sword, Asterio for the spear, and the bow’s…”

“Forshna.”

“Yeah. Forshna.”

Lark looked slightly annoyed at being cut off.

“I want to confirm something. That spirit bow is…”

“That beam—more like a laser weapon? Yeah. That thing is straight-up cheating.”

Even from what was known, it could casually punch through a tank, and the range was at least a kilometer.

On top of that, the wielder excelled at hiding their presence, and the Empire never managed to identify the owner to the end—an absurd explanation to be forced to swallow.

‘That monster is in the same Forshna clan as Number Six-san…’

It was two hundred years ago, so maybe they were already long dead, but with elves, it was entirely possible they were still alive—old like that grandpa, but alive.

Only now did I realize how dangerous a tightrope I’d been walking, and as I thanked my luck, I also swore to keep sexually harassing Number Six-san while being careful not to make her too angry.

Maybe he sensed something from my demeanor, because Lark burst out laughing—then spat blood.

“Hey!”

Blood spilled every time he coughed.

The amount was nothing like before.

I scooped Lark up with both arms, followed where he pointed, and slipped through a door.

I laid him down on the bed inside and asked, “Is there anything I can do?” but he only shook his head in silence.

His condition deteriorated rapidly.

No—more like the wall that had supported him finally collapsed.

Because I’d come. Because I’d told him.

I’d destroyed the reason he’d kept living.

I’d given him a reason to stop the days he’d endured through clenched teeth.

“Don’t… worry. I told you—‘a life that might end tomorrow.’”

“Don’t go making that a self-fulfilling prophecy.”

Lark gave a weak smile and opened his mouth as if squeezing out his last strength.

The fact that he still wanted to talk meant he really was a genuine chatterbox noble.

“This illness… is a symptom… of those who couldn’t… adapt… to magic… Little by little… you lose your senses… and now… I… don’t… ah… my vision’s blurring…”

I couldn’t get the words stop talking out.

All I could do was place his hand on top of my fingers.

Even while coughing and spitting blood, Lark kept trying to speak, but his voice gradually grew smaller, thinner.

Then his mouth moved, but no sound came out.

But he was still alive.

So I decided to talk in his stead.

“If you can’t speak, squeeze my fingers instead as an answer,” I said first—then continued from where I’d left off.

How I’d eaten another subject without knowing who they were.

How I’d fought in the Kingdom of Canaan, and how I’d teased mercenary groups for fun.

Each time I spoke, Lark’s hand weakly touched my fingers.

And when the story moved to him—the third subject, Inos—my gaze slipped away from Lark for just a moment.

The story continued, but his hand stayed resting on my fingers, and it didn’t move again.

+++

# **150**

I didn’t know if it could be called a burial.

But if I buried him in the soil and stacked stones that looked like they could become a grave, it certainly looked the part.

I closed my eyes, crossed myself before my chest, and offered a silent prayer.

It pained me that the flowers I offered were just whatever happened to be blooming nearby, but he seemed like the kind of man who would say that crossing over with a single wild flower in hand wasn’t so bad.

“I wanted to ask you so much more…”

There was so much I wanted to know.

So much I still wanted to ask.

But he—who must have already been at his limit—couldn’t hold on.

Meeting me was surely his miracle, not mine.

“I got a lot of information. I met a fellow survivor. I’ll call that enough.”

Turning my back on Lark’s grave—something no one could call well-made—I returned to the facility.

From what I’d seen, it was in remarkably good condition, so there was a high chance I’d adopt it as my main base.

If I could secure a way to keep warm, winter would stop being a concern, so I decided to thoroughly check the inside.

First, I needed to confirm what was going on with the electrical system.

I tried hard to operate the terminal at the reception desk that caught my eye, but with these fingers, the controls were clumsy and unreliable.

“Isn’t there something I can use?”

I set my pack down and rummaged through it, and found a pen and a ladle.

‘Of the things I have, these two are the only ones that might work.’

I hesitated over the underwhelming choice, but in the end I grabbed the pen.

Pinching it between my thumb and index finger, I tapped at the keyboard, clicking out inputs.

I figured I’d have it give me a facility guide, but unfortunately it wasn’t working properly—after a beep, it froze.

It was noisy, so I shut the power off, but were there any functioning devices at all?

About a minute after I worried that, I found an electronic guidance board that actually worked.

It was touch-panel, so even my fingers were fine.

First I confirmed the location info, and then I started by finding the map he’d left behind.

The room I wanted—Lark’s bedroom—was found quickly.

It was a room meant for humans, so getting inside took some effort, but I managed to wedge my body in by simply breaking the door.

Now then—where was it?

I pinched the desk drawers with my fingertips, yanked them open, and checked the contents one by one.

In the second drawer, I found a folded sheet of paper that looked promising, so I took it out and unfolded it.

“Ah… I get why you didn’t bother telling me.”

There really was a map, just like he’d said.

But the marks that seemed meant to indicate facility locations—aside from here—numbered only two.

One of those points was not far from here, but there was a high chance it was the facility with that octopus-type chimera.

And the other was probably the facility where the spider man had been—meaning, the place I’d been using as a base.

‘So there’s no haul… no, even so, I should go confirm it.’

It was worth that much. I carefully folded the map and put it into my pack.

There was a chance this wasn’t the only thing Lark had left, so I searched the room thoroughly, but there was no other map-like item.

Instead, I obtained an old lantern.

I couldn’t help thinking, Why would he have this?

But when I examined it more closely, the construction was rough—more like amateur work—so I settled on the conclusion that Lark had made it to go into places where the lights didn’t work.

In that case, I’d take it. I hung it from my pack.

With the shovel already there, I looked like some kind of laborer.

If I had a pickaxe, I’d be a miner.

Now then—I’d left the room, so it was time to check other areas.

To me, this was an unexplored facility.

I had no idea what was inside, so I needed to confirm everything with my own eyes.

So I explored the facility for a while, and found a kitchen, a storage room, and a game room.

When I said “kitchen,” it was really just a place Lark had used for that purpose—there wasn’t any noteworthy equipment tied to it.

Still, the moderately cleared space suited me too, and continuing to use it was an option.

When I rummaged around lightly, I found multiple traps that looked like they’d been used to catch small animals.

In addition, I found dried meat and what looked like edible wild plants in a space that seemed like a pantry.

I gratefully took them.

I wanted to complain about how bad the dried meat tasted, but I chewed it in silence while searching the storage room.

Unlike me, who carried back anything that seemed remotely usable, Lark had surprisingly packed only what he needed.

The problem was that most of it was stuff I didn’t need now, so there wasn’t much I could call a haul.

I didn’t have the guts to open a two-hundred-year-old can.

But if it was here… had Lark been eating it?

I tried opening one, but it was no good.

As for the game room… it was just something the researchers had once been provided and Lark used as-is, so there was nothing particularly valuable. I gave it a quick look and moved on.

“Hm. I don’t see a control room… He said there was another facility next door—maybe it’s over there?”

Considering Lark had been here, there was a chance the two were connected somewhere.

I decided to explore the areas I’d avoided because wedging my body through them was a hassle, and found a pitch-dark corridor.

‘There are lights… but the power isn’t on.’
